The Name of Faith is Established Before Sin
Maturidi proves the name 'believer' is given before sin via Quran and hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quran and hadith to prove that the name 'believer' is established before sin and not removed by it.

Core Idea
Faith is given before sin; sin does not remove the name of believer.

This passage presents Maturidi's scriptural argument that the title 'believer' is granted at the outset of faith, not earned by sinlessness. He cites key verses and a hadith to forbid denying faith to fellow Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow: a principle, then proofs, then conclusion.
2
Identify each scriptural proof
List the proofs Maturidi uses: two Qur'anic verses (2:136, 2:285), a prohibition verse (4:94), the hadith of Jibril, and the hadith of fighting.
Each proof supports the same conclusion.
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that such a person is a believer by the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and linguistic usage.
The conclusion ties all proofs together.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider the practical impact: sins do not negate faith; the name of believer remains until clear disbelief.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل في ذلك أن الله تعالى ألزمه اسم الايمان قبل ارتكابه مما ارتكب وأزال عنه اسم الكفر بقوله : قُولُوا آمَنَّا بِاللَّهِ ، وقوله : آمَنَ الرَّسُولُ ، فبين بما يكون المرء مؤمنا وحرم على من يقول لمثله : لست مؤمنا بقوله : وَلَا تَقُولُوا لِمَنْ أَلْقَى إِلَيْكُمْ السَّلَامَ لَسْتَ مُؤْمِنًا ، وبين رسول الله سأله جبريل عن الإيمان ، فبين ذلك وحقق له اسم المؤمن بإتيان ذلك ، وكذلك أُمِرْتُ أَنْ أُقاتِلَ النَّاسَ إلى آخر ما ذكر ، فهذا الآن مؤمن بالكتاب والسنة وما أجمعت عليه الأمة ، وما شهدت له أهل اللغة
Then the principle in that is that God obligated for him the name of faith (īmān) before his commission of what he committed and removed from him the name of disbelief (kufr) by His saying: 'Say: We believe in Allah' (2:136), and His saying: 'The Messenger believes' (2:285). So He made clear by what a person is a believer and forbade that one say to his like: 'You are not a believer' by His saying: 'And do not say to those who give you the greeting of peace, “You are not a believer”' (4:94). And the Messenger of God – when Gabriel asked him about faith, he explained that and affirmed for him the name of believer by his performing that. And likewise: 'I have been commanded to fight the people' until the end of what was mentioned. So this one is now a believer by the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus of the community, and what the people of language attest to.
Source pages p. 941

Commentary
This passage provides a scriptural proof that the name 'believer' (mu'min) is established before one commits any sin, and that sin does not remove it. The author cites several Qur'anic verses: 'Say: We believe in Allah' (2:136) and 'The Messenger believes' (2:285) – these are declarations of faith that apply to the believers as a group, including those who later sin. The verse 'And do not say to those who give you the greeting of peace, “You are not a believer”' (4:94) explicitly forbids denying the name of faith to someone who outwardly professes Islam, even if his interior state is unknown. The author also references the hadith of Gabriel (Jibrīl) where the Prophet explained the pillars of faith (īmān) to him, thereby affirming that anyone who fulfills those pillars is called a believer. The hadith 'I have been commanded to fight the people...' (referring to the famous hadith about fighting until they say 'La ilaha illallah') also indicates that utterance of the shahada grants the status of a believer. The author concludes that such a person is a believer according to the Qur'an, Sunnah, consensus (ijmā'), and the linguistic usage of the Arabs.

Key Terms
اسم الايمان
مؤمن
كفر
إيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that committing sins removes the name of believer entirely.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the name 'believer' is given before any sin and is not removed by sin, as evidenced by Quranic prohibitions against calling Muslims disbelievers.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does Maturidi use to forbid calling a fellow Muslim an unbeliever?
A) 'Say: We believe in Allah' (2:136)
B) 'The Messenger believes' (2:285)
C) 'And do not say to those who give you the greeting of peace, "You are not a believer"' (4:94)
D) 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be ascribed to Him' (4:48)

Key Takeaway
The name 'believer' is given by God before any sin and is not negated by subsequent sins; it is only removed by clear disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your sins do not remove your status as a believer? Or have you allowed shame over past failures to make you doubt that God still calls you a believer?
?

?
If the name of believer is given by God before sin, what does that mean for your identity? Are you living as one who has been named by God, or are you defined by your mistakes?

Fisq, 'Isyan, and Zulm: Not Contrary to Faith
Maturidi defines fisq, 'isyan, and zulm and asserts they are not opposites of iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of fisq, 'isyan, and zulm according to Imam Maturidi, and why they are not contrary to iman.

Core Idea
Fisq, 'isyan, and zulm do not negate faith; they are defined relative to command.

Imam Maturidi begins by clarifying three key terms that are often misunderstood in relation to faith. He provides precise lexical definitions for fisq, 'isyan, and zulm and shows that they do not contradict iman. This passage helps distinguish between sin and unbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice how Maturidi presents each definition concisely.
Pay attention to the three divisions of fisq.
2
Identify the three definitions
Find the definitions of fisq, zulm, and 'isyan. Note their key phrases: 'exiting from command', 'placing in other than proper place', 'opposition'.
Fisq has three subcategories; memorize them.
3
Understand the warning
Read the final sentence where Maturidi warns against conflating these acts with iman. Ask yourself why someone might think they negate faith.
This connects to the common misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المعاني التي هي للفسق والعصيان والظلم ليست بمضادة للإيمان ؛ إذ الفسق اسم الخروج عن الأمر ، وجائز ذلك على أقسام ثلاثة : يخرج مما هو أمر إرشاد وفرض واعتقاد، ، إذ هو وكذلك الظلم اسم لوضع الشيء غير موضعه ، والعصيان اسم للخلاف ، فمن رتب الكل في الجزاء أو فى حقيقة المعنى وأراد أن يزيد اسم الإيمان بكل ذلك فنفسه يظلم ، ولدفع ما فرق الله ورسوله والأئمة يتعرض ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the meanings that are for fisq (sinfulness), 'isyan (disobedience), and zulm (injustice) are not contrary to iman (faith). For fisq is the name for exiting from the command, and that is possible in three divisions: exiting from what is a command of guidance (irshād), obligation (farḍ), and belief (i'tiqād). Likewise, zulm is the name for placing a thing in other than its proper place, and 'isyan is the name for opposition. So whoever arranges all of them in recompense or in the reality of meaning and intends to increase the name of iman by all that, then he wrongs himself, and he exposes himself to opposing what God, His Messenger, and the imams have differentiated, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 942

Commentary
This passage provides lexical and theological definitions of three key terms: fisq (sinfulness), 'isyan (disobedience), and zulm (injustice). The author asserts that these are not opposites (muḍādda) of iman. He defines fisq as 'exiting from a command' (khurūj 'an al-amr), and notes that this can occur in three types: exiting from a command of guidance (irshād), an obligatory command (farḍ), or a command of belief (i'tiqād). The phrase 'إذ هو وكذلك' is awkward but likely means 'and likewise' before moving to zulm. Zulm is defined as 'placing a thing in other than its proper place' (waḍ' al-shay' ghayr mawḍi'ihi), and 'isyan as 'opposition' (khilāf). The author warns against conflating these with iman in terms of recompense or essential meaning; doing so would be self-injustice and would contradict the distinctions made by God, the Prophet, and the early imams. This underlines that these acts do not negate the name of faith, though they are sinful.

Key Terms

عصيان
ظلم
إيمان
خروج عن الأمر
إرشاد
فرض
اعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that sinfulness (fisq) is the opposite of faith, so committing a sin takes one out of iman.
Correction: Maturidi explains that fisq is merely exiting from a command, which can happen to a believer; it does not negate iman unless it involves rejection of belief.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the definition of 'zulm'?
A) Exiting from a command.
B) Placing a thing in other than its proper place.
C) Opposition to a command.
D) Believing in falsehood.

Key Takeaway
Fisq, 'isyan, and zulm are defined as exiting from command, misplacement, and opposition respectively, and they do not negate the name of iman.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This passage defines zulm as 'placing a thing in other than its proper place.' Where in your life are you currently placing something (your time, priorities, relationships) in a position that belongs only to Allah? What is the cost of that misplacement?

But have you ever tried to justify a sin by calling it 'just a small sin' or 'not that bad'? Al-Ka'bi's Claim on the Derivation of 'Believer'
Maturidi reports Al-Ka'bi's view on the derivation of 'believer'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ka'bi's argument that the name 'believer' is not derived from any act of obedience, but from a specific religious act.

Core Idea
The name 'believer' stems from a religious act, not just any obedience.

Maturidi presents Al-Ka'bi's view challenging the idea that 'believer' is simply derived from the act of affirming or obeying. Al-Ka'bi uses a striking analogy to show that names can contradict reality, arguing that the name 'believer' must come from a specific act of religious praise and differentiation. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the claim and the analogy used. Even though the text has unclear markings, the overall logic is discernible.
The [١] symbols likely refer to textual variants or missing words; focus on the argument structure.
2
Identify Al-Ka'bi's main claim
Find the sentence where he says 'our saying that one is a believer is not derived from the act.' Understand that he denies a general derivation from any act of affirmation or obedience.
3
Analyze the analogy
Al-Ka'bi compares calling a beautiful woman ugly to calling someone a believer who does not possess the proper attributes. This shows that the name can be applied without the reality matching.
The analogy is crucial to his argument that the name must be derived from something specific.
4
Understand the conclusion
He concludes that the name is derived from an act and praise in religion, and for differentiation. This means 'believer' is not a general term for any obedient person but a specific religious designation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وليس قولنا: مؤمن، بمشتق من الفعل به كل من يصدق أحدا، وأطاعه وخضع له اسما مطلقا، أيضا [١]سمه فقط؛ إذ لو كان [١]سمه الجاز التسمية به لمن ليس هو كما تسمى الحسناء قبيحة، وإذا لم يكن كذلك ثبت أنه اسم مشتق من فعل ومدح في الدين و[ا] سمه للتفرقة ولا هو كذلك.
He said: 'Our saying that one is a believer is not derived from the act, because everyone who affirms someone, obeys him, and submits to him is not absolutely named thereby; also [1] only; for if [1] were to permit naming someone who is not like that, just as a beautiful woman is called ugly. And if it is not like that, it is established that the name is derived from an act and praise in religion and [a] only for differentiation, and it is not like that.'
Source pages p. 942

Commentary
This passage is a report of Al-Ka'bi's argument against the derivation of 'believer' from the act of faith. He contends that the name 'believer' is not derived from the act of affirming or obeying, because many people affirm and obey but are not called believers. He likens it to calling a beautiful woman ugly—the name does not match the reality. He concludes that the name must be derived from an act specifically related to religious praise and differentiation, implying it is not simply from any obedient act.

Misconception: Al-Ka'bi denies any connection between actions and the name 'believer'.
Correction: Al-Ka'bi does not deny a connection; he argues that the name is derived from a specific religious act, not just any act of obedience.

Key Takeaway

The Name 'Believer' and Its Basis in Action
Maturidi analyzes Al-Ka'bi's statements on the derivation of 'believer'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of Al-Ka'bi's claim that the name 'believer' is not derived from the act of faith.

Core Idea
Maturidi shows that Al-Ka'bi's own logic leads to contradiction.

Maturidi begins by examining Al-Ka'bi's claim that every obedience is from faith but not the same as faith. He points out that Al-Ka'bi simultaneously admits that a major sinner retains the name of faith yet denies its full application. This sets the stage for a deeper inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice Maturidi's method of quoting then analysing.
2
Identify Al-Ka'bi's two statements
Find where Maturidi reports Al-Ka'bi's sayings: one about obedience and faith, another about the name being an act but not applied. Notice how Maturidi sees a contradiction between them.
3
Understand Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi argues that if Al-Ka'bi admits a major sinner still has the name of faith in some sense, then he cannot consistently deny the application of the name. Grasp how this undermines Al-Ka'bi's position.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله: نقول وبالله نستعين: قوله: أهل الحق كذا حق، إن لم يرد بقوله: كل طاعة من الإيمان، غير أنه من الإيمان، وهو كما يقول: كل نعمة من الله، أي به نيلت وكانت، وكذلك الإيمان. يستحق بكذا، فقد نقضه حيث زعم أن مع صاحب الكبيرة له الاسم. وقوله: اسم الإيمان فعل لكن لا يطلق، ووصف في هذا ما ذكر، وذلك عنده في التحقيق اسم المؤمن، [۱۷۹] لا اسم الإيمان؛ إذ يحققه بدونه ولا يسميه به، فبهذا مبلغ علمه بقوله. وقوله: ليس باسم مشتق من فعله، عجيب؛ إذ حقق لفعله ذلك الاسم، ثم منع التسمية به، وذلك يوجب جواز كل أحد بغير اسم فعله في الحقيقة ومنع التسمية به، وفي ذلك لزوم اسم كل فاعل غير فاعل، وكل من ليس بفاعل فاعلا، وكذا ذا في الحركة، ونحو ذلك.
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: We say, and with Allah we seek help: His statement, 'The people of truth are right if he did not intend by his statement that every obeyance is from faith' — rather it is from faith, and it is as one says: 'Every blessing is from Allah,' meaning by it it was attained and existed, and similarly faith. He is worthy of something, but he refuted it when he claimed that with the committer of a major sin the name belongs to him. And his statement: 'The name of faith is an act but it is not applied' — and he described in this what he mentioned, and that in his investigation is the name of the believer, not the name of faith; for he verifies it without it and does not name him by it. So this is the extent of his knowledge of his statement. And his statement: 'It is not a name derived from his act' is strange; for he confirmed for his act that name, then forbade naming by it, and that necessitates permitting anyone by other than the name of his act in reality and forbidding naming by it, and in that lies the necessity of the name of every doer being non-doer, and every non-doer being a doer, and likewise this in movement, and the like.
Source pages p. 943

Commentary
This passage begins Maturidi's refutation of Al-Ka'bi. He first notes a possible interpretation of 'every obedience is from faith' that Al-Ka'bi might accept, then points out a contradiction: Al-Ka'bi admits that a person with a major sin still retains the name of faith in some sense, yet he denies its application. Maturidi finds it strange that Al-Ka'bi confirms the act as the basis of the name but then withholds the name. This leads to absurd consequences: one would then have to call a doer a non-doer and vice versa.

Key Terms
اسم الإيمان
فعل
صاحب الكبيرة
تسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi is arguing that the name 'believer' is applied based on any act, regardless of faith.
Correction: Maturidi is not making a general claim; he is exposing Al-Ka'bi's inconsistency, not arguing for a free application of the name.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi identify as a contradiction in Al-Ka'bi's argument?
A) Al-Ka'bi denies that faith consists of acts
B) Al-Ka'bi admits the major sinner has the name but denies its application
C) Al-Ka'bi uses a false analogy
D) Al-Ka'bi ignores consensus

Key Takeaway
Maturidi exposes the contradiction in Al-Ka'bi's position: admitting a basis for the name while forbidding its use.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim committing a major sin, do you hesitate to call them a believer? What does Maturidi's critique of Al-Ka'bi's inconsistency reveal about your own heart? Pledge to examine whether you separate theoretical affirmation from practical judgment.
?
This passage shows how even learned scholars can hold contradictory views about others. Where in your life do you affirm a principle ('every blessing is from Allah') but deny its application ('this person is not worthy')? Commit to aligning your speech with your beliefs.
?
Maturidi says Al-Ka'bi 'verifies it without it'. Are you guilty of acknowledging the basis for someone's faith but withholding the name due to their sin? How will you change this starting today?

The Absurdity of Denying the Name Despite Its Basis
Maturidi uses a reductio ad absurdum to refute Al-Ka'bi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's reductio ad absurdum argument against Al-Ka'bi's claim that the name 'believer' is not derived from the act.

Core Idea
Consistency demands that if the act grounds the name, the name must be applied.

Maturidi continues his refutation by highlighting the strangeness of Al-Ka'bi's position. If the act of faith is the basis for the name, then withholding the name leads to absurd consequences: we would have to call a doer a non-doer and vice versa. This argument shows the necessity of deriving the name from the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for force
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice Maturidi's tone of astonishment at Al-Ka'bi's inconsistency.
The word 'عجيب' (strange) signals the reductio.
2
Identify the reductio
Maturidi argues that if Al-Ka'bi confirms the act gives the name but then forbids naming, then one could justifiably call a doer a non-doer, and a non-doer a doer. Understand this logical consequence.
3
Apply to movement analogy
Maturidi extends the argument to other attributes like movement. This shows the principle is general.
This strengthens the refutation by showing the absurdity extends beyond the specific case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: We say, and with Allah we seek help: His statement, 'The people of truth are right if he did not intend by his statement that every obeyance is from faith' — rather it is from faith, and it is as one says: 'Every blessing is from Allah,' meaning by it it was attained and existed, and similarly faith. He is worthy of something, but he refuted it when he claimed that with the committer of a major sin the name belongs to him. And his statement: 'The name of faith is an act but it is not applied' — and he described in this what he mentioned, and that in his investigation is the name of the believer, not the name of faith; for he verifies it without it and does not name him by it. Source pages p. 943

This passage begins Maturidi's refutation of Al-Ka'bi. He first notes a possible interpretation of 'every obedience is from faith' that Al-Ka'bi might accept, then points out a contradiction: Al-Ka'bi admits that a person with a major sin still retains the name of faith in some sense, yet he denies its application. Misconception: Some might think Maturidi is arguing for literal derivation of every name from actions.

Maturidi's Refutation of Al-Ka'bi's Consensus Claim
Maturidi refutes Al-Ka'bi's claim of consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes Al-Ka'bi's claim of consensus regarding naming sinners, and the logical analogy he uses.

Core Idea
Consensus cannot override the literal application of a name based on ignorance of intent.

In this lesson, we examine a key passage from Kitab al-Tawhid where Imam Maturidi confronts Al-Ka'bi's assertion that the Muslim community unanimously agrees a sinner should not be called a believer. Maturidi dissects this claim into two falsehoods and provides a powerful analogy to clarify the correct principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify Al-Ka'bi's claim
Read the passage and identify the two-part claim Al-Ka'bi makes: that the name is not applied and that there is consensus on declaring wrong anyone who does so.
2
Understand Maturidi's two refutation points
Maturidi calls it a lie in two respects. Find those two: (1) the name is indeed applied, but the intended meaning is unknown; (2) the consensus claim is false.
3
Analyze the analogy
Maturidi compares faith to obedience: we do not say 'so-and-so obeyed absolutely' without knowing the intent, but it is still obedience. Apply this to faith.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that when knowledge is attained, the statement becomes necessary. Contrast with the case of an unbeliever who is called a falsifier because his reality is known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قال من قوله: صدق كذا، لا يسمى بذلك، واتبع ذلك قوله: أجمع أهل الملة تخطئة من فعل ذلك، كذب من وجهين: أحدهما أنه قال: لا يطلق له الاسم، بل هو مطلق له، لكن لا يعرف ما يراد بذلك الإيمان، فلهذا يجب تثبيته لا لأن ذلك ليس باسم له، وكذلك لا يقال: أطاع فلان مطلقا بما أطاع أحد من حيث لا يعرف المراد، لا لأنه لم يستحق يفعله الإطلاق، ولكنه طاعة من لا يعرف الأمر به، وكذلك الإيمان، فإذا صار إلى المعرفة لزم القول، وكذلك لا يقال: فلان مصدق أو مكذب بما كان منه ذلك في أحد حتى يتبين، ثم كل من كفر بالله يقال: مكذب؛ لما عرف حقيقته، فمثله المؤمن، والله الموفق.
And what he said from his statement: 'He affirmed such-and-such, he is not called by that' — and he followed that with his statement: 'The people of the religion are agreed in declaring wrong the one who does that' — is a lie in two respects: one of them is that he said the name is not applied to him, rather it is applied to him, but one does not know what is intended by that faith, therefore it must be affirmed, not because it is not a name for him. And similarly it is not said: 'So-and-so obeyed absolutely' by his obeying someone, because the intended meaning is not known, not because he does not deserve the absolute term, but it is the obedience of one who does not know the command with it. And likewise faith: when one arrives at knowledge, the statement becomes necessary. And similarly it is not said: 'So-and-so is a verifier or falsifier' by what he did from that in someone until it becomes clear. Then everyone who disbelieves in Allah is called a falsifier because his reality is known. Similarly the believer. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 943

Commentary
In this passage, Maturidi attacks Al-Ka'bi's appeal to consensus (ijma). He accuses him of lying in two ways. First, Al-Ka'bi claims the name is not applied, but in fact it is applied, though the full meaning is not always recognized. Second, he misrepresents the community's view. Maturidi draws an analogy: we do not say 'so-and-so obeyed absolutely' without knowing the intention, but it is still obedience. Similarly, we may withhold the name 'believer' due to ignorance of intent, not because the name is inapplicable. He compares this to calling an unbeliever 'mukadhdhib' (falsifier) because his reality is known.

Key Terms
إجماع
تخطئة
اسم
إيمان
معرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Maturidi rejects the concept of consensus entirely.
Correction: Maturidi refutes a specific misuse of consensus by Al-Ka'bi, not the principle of consensus itself.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi respond to Al-Ka'bi's claim that the community agrees on not naming a sinner a believer?
A) He accepts the consensus
B) He says the name is not applied at all
C) He says the name is applied but the meaning is unknown
D) He says the community has no consensus on this

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the name 'believer' can be applied even when full knowledge is absent, and consensus cannot be used to deny that.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi says we do not call someone a verifier or falsifier until the matter becomes clear. Is there an area in your own faith where you have prematurely judged yourself or others? How does this passage call you to refrain from hasty labeling?
?
The analogy of obedience—obeying without knowing the full command—is still called obedience. In what part of your life have you acted on faith without full understanding, and how can you take comfort that your imperfect obedience is still counted?
?
Al-Ka'bi used consensus to dismiss a whole category of believers. When have you used the opinion of the majority to dismiss or doubt your own faith? How can you instead affirm your faith while seeking to understand its full intent?

The Contradiction in Denying 'Iman' as a Derivative Name
Maturidi refutes the opponent's self-contradictory denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical refutation of the claim that 'iman' is not a name derived from its action, and the absurd consequences that follow.

Core Idea
Denying the name while affirming the act leads to calling agents non-agents.

Maturidi begins by exposing a logical flaw in the opponent's position. The opponent admits that the name 'iman' refers to an action but refuses to apply it to the believer. Maturidi shows that this creates a contradiction: it would require us to deny people the names of their actual deeds, leading to absurd reversals like calling an agent a non-agent. This passage grounds the discussion in rational consistency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Read the first part of the passage where the opponent states that the name of iman is an action but is not applied. Notice Maturidi's initial response highlighting the contradiction.
The opponent's position is self-contradictory: he admits the act has a name but denies the person that name.
2
Understand the logical consequence
Maturidi argues that if the opponent's position were followed, it would permit calling every person by a name contrary to their actual action. This is the heart of the refutation.
This consequence is absurd in rational terms.
3
See the absurd reversal
Maturidi explicitly states that the opponent's view would require us to call an agent a non-agent and a non-agent an agent, and similarly for movement and other actions. This shows the untenability of the opponent's position.
The absurdity is meant to expose the logical flaw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : اسم الإيمان فعل لكن لا يطلق ، ووصف في هذا ما ذكر ، وذلك عنده في التحقيق اسم المؤمن لا اسم الإيمان ؛ إذ يحققه بدونه ولا يسميه به ، فبهذا مبلغ علمه بقوله . وقوله : ليس باسم مشتق من فعله ، عجيب ؛ إذ حقق لفعله ذلك الاسم ، ثم منع التسمية به ، وذلك يوجب جواز كل أحد بغير اسم فعله في الحقيقة ومنع التسمية به ، وفى ذلك لزوم اسم كل فاعل غير فاعل ، وكل من ليس بفاعل فاعلا ، وكذا ذا في الحركة ، ونحو ذلك ، وما قال من قوله : صدق كذا ، لا يسمى بذلك ، واتبع ذلك قوله : أجمع أهل الملة تخطئة من فعل ذلك ، كذب من وجهين : أحدهما أنه قال : لا يطلق له الاسم ، بل هو مطلق له ، لكن لا يعرف ما يراد بذلك الإيمان ، فلهذا يجب تثبيته لا لأن ذلك ليس باسم له ، وكذلك لا يقال : أطاع فلان مطلقا بما أطاع أحد من حيث لا يعرف المراد ، لا لأنه لم يستحق يفعله الإطلاق ، ولكنه طاعة من لا يعرف الأمر به، وكذلك الإيمان ، فإذا صار إلى المعرفة لزم القول ، وكذلك لا يقال : فلان مصدق أو مكذب بما كان منه ذلك في أحد حتى يتبين ، ثم كل من كفر بالله يقال : مكذب ؛ لما عرف حقيقته ، فمثله المؤمن ، والله الموفق .
His saying: The name of iman is an action but it is not applied, and he described in this what was mentioned, and that in his view is in reality the name of the believer, not the name of iman; because he realizes it without it and does not name him by it. So this is the extent of his knowledge by his statement. And his saying: It is not a name derived from its action is strange; for he confirmed for its action that name, then forbade naming by it, and that necessitates permitting every person without the name of his action in reality and forbidding naming by it, and in that there is the necessity of the name of every agent as non-agent, and every non-agent as agent, and likewise concerning movement and the like. And what he said: Such and such is true, it is not named by that, and he followed that with his saying: The people of the religion are agreed in declaring wrong whoever does that. This is a lie in two respects: one is that he said: The name is not applied to it, rather it is applied to him, but one does not know what is intended by that iman, so it is necessary to affirm it, not because that is not a name for him. Similarly, it is not said: So-and-so obeyed absolutely by obeying someone because one does not know the intention, not because he did not deserve to have the expression applied, but it is obedience of one who does not know the command. And likewise iman, so when it comes to knowledge, the statement becomes necessary. Similarly, it is not said: So-and-so is a verifier or falsifier by what was from him in someone until it is clear. Then everyone who disbelieves in God is called a falsifier because his reality is known. The believer is like that. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 943

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent who claims that the name 'iman' (faith) is an action but is not actually applied as a name. Maturidi points out the contradiction: the opponent admits that the act has a name but then denies naming the person by it. He shows that this leads to absurd consequences, such as calling an agent a non-agent and vice versa. The opponent then claims consensus against applying the name, but Maturidi says this claim is false in two ways: first, the name is indeed applied, but the intended meaning is unknown; second, analogies with obedience and verification/disbelief show that once the reality is known, the name is applied. The believer is like the disbeliever in that both are named based on known reality.

Key Terms
اسم الإيمان
المؤمن
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the opponent's denial is harmless semantic distinction.
Correction: Maturidi shows it leads to logical absurdities, proving it is not harmless.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does Maturidi derive from the opponent's position?
A) The name of iman is unknown.
B) An agent would have to be called a non-agent, and vice versa.
C) The believer cannot be called a believer.
D) The opponent's argument is based on consensus.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that denying the derived name while affirming the action leads to logical inversion, undermining the opponent's position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call yourself a believer, do you examine whether your actions actually match that name? This lesson shows that denying the name while doing the act is contradictory. Are there any actions in your life that you do but hesitate to claim the identity that comes with them? What would it take for you to fully own the name 'believer' by consistently acting on iman?
?
Maturidi's argument reveals that separating the name of an action from its agent leads to logical absurdity. Are there areas in your faith where you separate belief from action, thinking you can have iman without fully embodying it? How can you close that gap starting today?

Refuting the Claim of Consensus on 'Iman'
Maturidi refutes the opponent's consensus claim and provides analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the opponent's argument that there is a consensus against applying the name 'iman', and the analogy with obedience and falsification.

Core Idea
The name is applied, but the meaning may be unknown; once known, the name sticks.

The opponent tries to bolster his case by claiming that the people of the religion unanimously deem it wrong to apply the name 'iman' in the way Maturidi suggests. Maturidi counters that this claim is false on two counts: first, the name is indeed applied, albeit with incomplete understanding; second, analogies from obedience and falsification show that naming depends on knowledge of the reality. This passage clarifies the relationship between naming and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the opponent's consensus argument
Read the part where the opponent claims that the people of the religion agree in declaring wrong the action of applying the name 'iman'. Understand that this is meant to support his denial.
The opponent appeals to consensus as authority.
2
Understand Maturidi's two-part refutation
Maturidi says the claim is a lie in two respects: (1) the name is applied, but the intended meaning is unknown; (2) the consensus claim is based on ignorance of the meaning. Focus on the first refutation.
Maturidi does not deny the consensus; he reinterprets it.
3
See the analogy with obedience and falsification
Maturidi uses analogies: obedience is not called obedience if the command is unknown, but once known, it is. Similarly, a disbeliever is called a falsifier because his reality is known. This reinforces that naming depends on knowledge of the reality.
The analogy shows that the name 'iman' should be applied once the reality is known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A) The opponent says the name is not applied, but it is; and the opponent says consensus is impossible.

Maturidi on Naming the Fasiq
Maturidi argues about naming the fasiq and exposes opponent's inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's nuanced position on whether the fasiq is called a believer and why he refrains from naming him.

Core Idea
Maturidi neither affirms nor denies the fasiq's faith—he refrains from naming him.

This passage from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid tackles a sensitive theological question: what do we call a Muslim who commits grave sins? Different sects had conflicting answers. Maturidi presents a subtle argument that avoids both extremes, showing that the opponent's positions lead to self-contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's argument sink in.
Notice the three options presented to the opponent.
2
Identify the three possible positions
The passage presents three possible stances on the fasiq: believer, not believer, or neither. Spot them in the text.
Maturidi discusses the consequences of each choice.
3
Understand the consequences of each choice
If the opponent says the fasiq is a believer, he contradicts himself because he avoids calling him one. If he says not believer or neither, he falsifies himself. Follow the reasoning.
This reveals the opponent's inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : لا نقول فى الفاسق عند التحقيق إنه ليس بمؤمن ، بل لا نسميه فيقال له : هو في التحقيق مؤمن أو ليس بمؤمن أو لا مؤمن ولا كافر . قال بالأول فهو رجل دعته نفسه إلى تكذيبها فيما هى ليست بكاذبة ، فأطاعها مثله الإغضاء عنه ؛ لأنه دون كل مقلد . فإن قال بالوجهين الآخرين فقد الكذب نفسه فيما حكى عنها ، والله الموفق .
Then he said: We do not say concerning the fasiq in reality that he is not a believer, rather we do not name him. So it is said to him: In reality is he a believer, or not a believer, or neither a believer nor a disbeliever? If he says the first, then he is a man whose soul called him to falsify it in what it is not false, so he obeyed it. Like that is overlooking him, because he is beneath every imitator. If he says the other two ways, then he has falsified himself in what he narrated about it. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 944

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the status of the grave sinner (fasiq) concerning the name 'believer'. Maturidi states that they do not say the fasiq is not a believer in reality, but they do not call him a believer. The opponent is then asked to choose among three positions: the fasiq is a believer, not a believer, or neither believer nor disbeliever. If he chooses the first (believer), he contradicts himself because he avoids calling him a believer. If he chooses the other two (not believer or intermediate), he falsifies his own claim that the fasiq is not a believer. Maturidi implies that the opponent's position is incoherent.

Key Terms
الفاسق
مؤمن
كافر
المقلد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi denies the fasiq is a believer absolutely.
Correction: Maturidi refrains from naming the fasiq a believer but does not say he is not a believer; 'we do not say he is not a believer'.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, if the opponent says the fasiq is not a believer in reality, what consequence does he face?
A) He has contradicted himself.
B) He has falsified himself.
C) He has agreed with Maturidi.
D) He has committed a sin.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refrains from calling the fasiq a believer or disbeliever, arguing that to assert either leads to self-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a person committing a major sin, do you find it easy to call them a 'disbeliever' in your heart? What does Maturidi's argument teach you about the danger of extreme judgments?
?
Maturidi refrains from naming the fasiq. Are there people in your life you have labeled as 'not true believers' because of their sins? How does this lesson challenge you to leave their ultimate status to God?
?
The opponent's inconsistency stemmed from avoiding one label while implying another. Where in your own speech about others do you subtly categorize them in ways that contradict your stated beliefs? What step can you take today to align your words with your theology?

All Acts of Worship Are Iman: The Quranic Proof from Surah Al-Imran
Maturidi uses Quran 3:85 to prove all acts of worship are iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses Quran 3:85 to establish that every act of worship is considered iman because it belongs to Islam.

Core Idea
Acts of worship are iman because they are accepted only through Islam.

Imam Maturidi presents a key argument for the unity of iman and acts of worship. Building on the earlier discussion that all acts of obedience are part of the religion, he turns to a powerful Quranic proof. In this passage, he cites verse 3:85 to show that any act of worship outside Islam is rejected, and therefore all accepted acts are within Islam and qualify as iman.

Guided Path
1
Set the Context
Remember that Maturidi is arguing that all acts of obedience (ta'at) are called iman because they belong to the religion (din).
This lesson focuses on his Quranic proof.
2
Read the Quranic Verse
Read Quran 3:85: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will not be accepted from him.'
This is the central evidence.
3
Follow the Reasoning
Maturidi explains that any act of worship is accepted only if it is within Islam. Since Islam is the same as iman, acts of worship are therefore part of iman.
Notice the logical connection: acceptance implies inclusion.
4
Understand the Objection and Counter
Some might object that not everything attributed to something takes its name. Maturidi counters with the example of blessings from God (Q 16:53), showing that the source does determine the nature.
This strengthens the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم استدل على تسمية جميع الطاعات إيمانا بما هي من الدين عند الجميع ، قوله : وَمَنْ يَتْبَعْ غَيْرَ الإِسْلَامِ دِينًا ، وقد بينا بعد من تعلق بالآية عن ما لا ريب أن مبتغى شيء من العبادات على الإشارة إليها بلا تقاد الإسلام دينا ، غير مقبول منه ، وإنما تقبل كل عبادة بدين الإسلام ، عبادة مشار إليها بها يقبل كل عبادة ولقوتها يرد ، وهذا معنى قول من الذين ، ولا كل شيء يضاف إلى شيء بأنه منه يجب له اسمه . قال تعالى : وَمَا بِكُم مِنْ نِعْمَةٍ فَمِنَ اللَّهِ لا أنها فى هو ، بل في ذلك دليل غيرية الدين حتى يضاف إليه ، فأقام به بعد الفراغ منه ، والله الموفق
Then he argued for naming all acts of obedience 'iman' on the basis that they are part of the religion according to everyone, by His saying: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will not be accepted from him' (Q 3:85). And we have explained afterwards that whoever clings to the verse is beyond doubt that the seeker of any act of worship in reference to it without taking Islam as a religion is not accepted from him. Rather every act of worship is accepted only by the religion of Islam; an act of worship is referred to by which every act of worship is accepted and by its strength it is rejected. And this is the meaning of the saying of those who say: Not everything that is attributed to something by being from it necessarily acquires its name. He said, exalted: 'And whatever blessing you have is from God' (Q 16:53), not that it is from Him, but rather in that there is evidence of the otherness of the religion until it is attributed to it. So he established it after having finished from it. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 944

Commentary
This passage is a proof that all acts of obedience (ta'at) are called iman because they belong to the religion (din). Maturidi cites Quran 3:85, which states that seeking a religion other than Islam is unacceptable. He argues that any act of worship is only accepted if it is within Islam. Therefore, since acts of worship are accepted only through Islam, and Islam is the same as iman, those acts are part of iman. He also addresses a potential objection: not everything attributed to something necessarily takes its name, but he uses the analogy of blessings from God (Q 16:53) to show that the source indicates the nature of the thing.

Key Terms
الطاعات
الإيمان
الدين
الإسلام
العبادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Acts of worship are separate from iman and should not be called iman.
Correction: Maturidi argues they are part of iman because they are accepted only through Islam, which is identical to iman.

Checkpoint
What is the main Quranic evidence Maturidi uses to argue that all acts of worship are iman?
A) Q 16:53
B) Q 3:85
C) Q 33:47
D) Q 9:100

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that every act of worship is iman because it is accepted only under the umbrella of Islam, which is identical to iman.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You perform many acts of worship daily—prayer, charity, fasting. If one of these was done merely as a habit, without consciously submitting to Allah through Islam, would it still be iman? Examine your prayer right now: Is it truly an act of iman, connected to your Islam, or has it become a hollow routine? What will you change before your next salah to make it a genuine act of iman?
?
Think of a good deed you did recently that was motivated by something other than Allah—perhaps recognition from others. According to this lesson, that deed is not accepted as part of Islam, and thus not iman. How many of your 'good deeds' are actually treasured with Allah? Commit today to purify your intentions, so that every act becomes part of your iman.
?
Maturidi teaches that every act of worship is accepted only through Islam. Is your life's orientation truly Islam, or are there areas—your work, relationships, ambitions—where you seek fulfillment outside the framework of submission to Allah? What would it mean to truly surrender every action to Allah? What is one area you can bring under the banner of Islam starting now?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
4 min
The fasiq is excluded from the believer's promise
Maturidi argues that reward verses exclude the fasiq from being a true believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the verse of glad tidings to argue that the fasiq (grave sinner) is not a true believer, and why this distinction is important for the definition of iman.

Core Idea
The fasiq is excluded from the believer's reward, showing he is not a true believer.

Imam Maturidi engages with a key Qur'anic verse that promises reward to 'the believers.' He points out that this verse does not include the fasiq, indicating that committing grave sins removes one from the category of true believers. This lesson unpacks his argument and the underlying consensus and disagreement among scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the verse citation and the conclusion drawn from it.
The verse is Q 33:47, which promises 'a great bounty' to the believers.
2
Identify the two positions
Maturidi distinguishes between the promise (universal for believers) and the threat (disputed). The fasiq is excluded from the promise.
Consensus exists on the universality of the promise, but not on the threat.
3
Reflect on the argument's significance
Consider why this matters: if the fasiq is excluded from the promise, he cannot be considered a true believer in the absolute sense.
This supports the view that faith is diminished by major sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج بقوله : وَبَشِّرِ المُؤْمِنِينَ بِأَنَّ لَهُم مِنَ اللَّهِ فَلا كَبِيرًا ، ونحو ذلك الوعيد للمؤمنين باسم المطلق ، ثم لم يكن ذلك لمرتكب الفسق فيه ، وقد أجمع إلى عموم الوعد وإن اختلف في عموم الوعيد . قال الشيخ رحمه الله : نقول وبالله التوفيق : دعواه الإجماع في عموم آيات
Then he argued by His saying: 'And give glad tidings to the believers that they will have a great bounty from God' (Q 33:47), and similar threats to believers by the absolute name, then that was not for the committer of fasiq therein, and they have agreed on the universality of the promise although they differed on the universality of the threat. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: We say, and with God is success: His claim of consensus concerning the universality of the verses...
Source pages p. 944

Commentary
This passage is an argument using verses that promise reward to believers. Maturidi points out that these verses address believers by the absolute name (al-mu'minin) and promise them a great reward. However, the committer of fasiq (grave sin) is not included in this promise, indicating that the fasiq is not considered a believer in the full sense. He notes that there is consensus on the universality of the promise (that all believers will receive it) but disagreement on the universality of the threat (whether all sinners are threatened). The Shaykh (Maturidi) then refutes the opponent's claim of consensus, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
المؤمنون
الوعد
الوعيد
الفسق
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The promise of reward applies to everyone who claims to be a believer, regardless of their actions.
Correction: Maturidi uses the verse to show that the fasiq is excluded from the promise, meaning that not all who claim belief are true believers; major sins remove one from that category.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the fact that reward verses address 'the believers' and the fasiq is not included?
A) The fasiq is a believer but does not deserve reward.
B) The fasiq is not a believer in the full sense.
C) The verses are abrogated.
D) The promise is only for the righteous.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the fasiq is excluded from the promise of reward given to believers, indicating that he is not a true believer in the absolute sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you claim to be among 'the believers' whom God promises a great bounty, how does your life reflect that identity? The verse explicitly excludes the fasiq—are there any major sins you are persisting in that would remove you from this promise?
?
This lesson shows that Maturidi used a verse of glad tidings to draw a stark line: the fasiq is not a true believer. When you read the Qur'an's promises and warnings, do you honestly apply them to yourself, or do you assume they apply to others?
?
The disagreement about the universality of threat (wa'id) reveals that even scholars differed on how to understand God's warnings. How do you respond to the tension between God's promise and His threat in your own heart? Commit to one concrete action this week that moves you from being careless about sin to being a true believer who avoids fisq.

The Status of the Grave Sinner: A Middle Path
Maturidi presents a dialectical exchange on the fasiq's belief status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's nuanced position on the grave sinner's belief status and why he avoids both extremes.

Core Idea
The grave sinner is neither fully declared a believer nor denied faith—a middle path.

Maturidi engages in a dialectical exchange to clarify the precise status of the grave sinner (fasiq). The passage presents a hypothetical question that forces a choice between three options, and then critiques each extreme. This lesson will unpack his reasoning and show why the correct stance is to refrain from naming him a believer while acknowledging his underlying faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the English translation. Notice the dialogical structure: a statement, a question, and a response.
2
Identify the three possible positions
The questioner offers three possibilities: he is a believer in reality, he is not a believer, or neither. Trace how the author responds to each.
The passage uses 'la nusammihi' meaning we do not name him.
3
Understand the critique of extremes
Maturidi argues that calling him a believer in reality leads to self-contradiction, while denying his faith amounts to lying about what the conscience knows. The middle way is to avoid naming him a believer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : لا نقول فى الفاسق عند التحقيق إنه ليس بمؤمن، بل لا نسميه. فيقال له : هو في التحقيق مؤمن أو ليس بمؤمن أو لا مؤمن ولا كافر. قال بالأول فهو رجل دعته نفسه إلى تكذيبها فيما هى ليست بكاذبة، فأطاعها مثله الإغضاء عنه؛ لأنه دون كل مقلد. فإن قال بالوجهين الآخرين فقد الكذب نفسه فيما حكى عنها، والله الموفق.
Then he said: We do not say regarding the fasiq (grave sinner) upon investigation that he is not a believer, but rather we do not name him. So it is said to him: Is he in reality a believer or not a believer, or neither a believer nor an unbeliever? If he says the first, then he is a man whose soul called him to deny it regarding what it is not lying about, so he obeyed it, and likewise overlooking him because he is below every blind follower. If he says the other two aspects, then he has lied regarding what he reported about it, and God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 944

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange addressing the precise theological status of the grave sinner (fasiq). The author first states the position: 'We do not say that the fasiq is not a believer in reality, but we do not call him a believer.' This is a nuanced middle position. Then a hypothetical questioner asks: 'Is he in reality a believer, or not a believer, or neither believer nor unbeliever?' The author answers: if the questioner chooses the first option (that he is a believer in reality), then he is contradicting himself because his own soul knows it is not lying, yet he follows its whim—this is compared to a blind follower (muqallid). If he chooses either of the other two options (that he is not a believer, or neither), then he has lied about what his soul reports, because his inner conscience knows the truth. Thus, the correct position is to avoid calling him a believer in name while acknowledging his underlying faith, so as to avoid both outright denial and false declaration.

Key Terms
فاسق
مؤمن
كافر
مقلد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The grave sinner (fasiq) is either a complete believer or a complete unbeliever with no intermediate status.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the fasiq still possesses faith (iman) in reality, but we should not call him a believer in name due to his grave sin. He is not an unbeliever, but we refrain from labeling him a believer publicly.

Checkpoint
What is the author's position on calling the fasiq a believer in reality?
A) He is a believer in reality.
B) He is not a believer in reality.
C) We do not say he is not a believer, but we do not name him as such.
D) He is neither believer nor unbeliever.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi navigates a middle path: the grave sinner retains faith but should not be called a believer in name, avoiding both extremes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim committing a major sin, do you silently begin to doubt the reality of their faith? Does this lesson convict you of a tendency to judge or label them? Will you now resist that urge and affirm their faith while still condemning the sin?
?
Think of a specific sin you committed while knowing deep down that you still believed in Allah. What kept you from denying your faith? How can that inner witness—your soul's knowledge—become a shield for you against despair and self-condemnation?
?
Are you sometimes like the blind follower described, obeying your soul's call to deny the truth about someone's faith because it is easier or more comfortable? What will you do the next time you are tempted to label a sinner as less of a believer?

All Acts of Obedience Are Part of Islam
Maturidi uses Quran 3:85 and an analogy to argue that all acts of obedience are iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi argues all acts of obedience are part of Islam (iman) using Quran 3:85 and clarifies that mere attribution to religion does not automatically give them the name 'iman'.

Core Idea
Acts of obedience are accepted only within Islam, but not every attributed act takes the name.

Imam Maturidi begins his proof that all acts of obedience are called iman by citing the verse 'Whoever seeks other than Islam as a religion...' (3:85). He then explains a crucial nuance: not everything that is part of something automatically shares its name. This lesson unpacks that argument and its significance for understanding iman.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi uses a Quranic verse as his starting point.
The verse is about the rejection of other religions.
2
Identify the main proof
Find how Maturidi uses verse 3:85 to show that all acts of obedience are part of Islam and thus called iman.
He argues that any act of worship is only accepted if done within the religion of Islam.
3
Understand the clarification about attribution
Maturidi then clarifies that not everything attributed to something takes its name. He uses the example of blessings from Allah (16:53) to show that 'from' does not mean identity.
This prevents a misunderstanding that every act of obedience automatically gets the name 'iman'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument establishes that iman includes all acts of obedience, but the naming depends on context and proper attribution.
This is a foundational point for later discussions on faith and deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم استدل على تسمية جميع الطاعات إيمانا بما هي من الدين عند الجميع، قوله : وَمَنْ يَتْبَعْ غَيْرَ الإِسْلَامِ دِينًا، وقد بينا بعد من تعلق بالآية عن ما لا ريب أن مبتغى شيء من العبادات على الإشارة إليها بلا تقيد الإسلام دينا، غير مقبول منه، وإنما تقبل كل عبادة بدين الإسلام، عبادة مشار إليها بها يقبل كل عبادة ولقوتها يرد، وهذا معنى قول من الدين، ولا كل شيء يضاف إلى شيء بأنه منه يجب له اسمه. قال تعالى : وَمَا بِكُم مِنْ نِعْمَةٍ فَمِنَ اللَّهِ لا أنها في هو، بل في ذلك دليل غيرية الدين حتى يضاف إليه، فأقام به بعد الفراغ منه، والله الموفق ثم احتج بقوله : وَبَشِّرِ المُؤْمِنِينَ بِأَنَّ لَهُم مِنَ اللَّهِ فَلا كَبِيرًا، ونحو ذلك الوعيد للمؤمنين باسم المطلق، ثم لم يكن ذلك لمرتكب الفسق فيه، وقد أجمع إلى عموم الوعد وإن اختلف في عموم الوعيد.
Then he used as evidence for calling all acts of obedience 'iman'—because they are part of religion according to all—His statement: 'And whoever seeks other than Islam as a religion...' [3:85]. And we have already explained after [that] regarding the one who clings to the verse that there is no doubt that the seeker of something from the acts of worship, indicating them without restriction to Islam as a religion, is not accepted from him. Rather, every act of worship is accepted only through the religion of Islam. The worship indicated by it accepts every act of worship and by its strength it is rejected. This is the meaning of the saying 'from the religion', and not everything that is attributed to something as being from it necessitates its name. He said, Exalted: 'And whatever of blessings you have is from Allah' [16:53]—not that it is in Him—rather in that is evidence of the otherness of the religion so that it is attributed to Him. Then he established it after completing it, and God is the Grantor of success. Then he argued with His statement: 'And give good tidings to the believers that they will have from Allah great bounty' [33:47], and similar threats to the believers by the absolute name. Then that was not for the committer of fasiq in it, and it is agreed upon the generality of the promise even if they differ on the generality of the threat.
Source pages p. 944

Commentary
This passage is a proof that all acts of obedience are called iman. The author first cites the verse 'Whoever seeks other than Islam as a religion...' to show that deeds are only accepted when done within Islam. He then clarifies that not everything attributed to something automatically takes its name—for example, not every act of obedience is called iman by the mere fact of being from the religion. He then uses the verse 'And whatever of blessings you have is from Allah' to illustrate that the attribution of deeds to God does not mean they are identical to Him; rather, it shows distinctness. Finally, he cites the promise to believers (33:47) and notes that threats are also addressed to believers by the absolute name 'believers', but the fasiq is not included in that pure promise. There is consensus on the general promise, though disagreement on the generality of the threat. Thus, the acts of obedience are part of iman, but the fasiq's status is nuanced.

Key Terms
إيمان
طاعات
دين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every act of obedience is automatically and invariably called iman just because it is part of the religion.
Correction: Not everything attributed to something shares its name. While acts of obedience are from the religion (iman), the name 'iman' is not mechanically applied to every individual act without consideration of context and proper attribution.

Checkpoint
What is the main function of the verse 'And whatever of blessings you have is from Allah' (16:53) in Maturidi's argument?
A) To prove that blessings are part of Allah's essence.
B) To show that not everything that is 'from' something is identical to that thing in name.
C) To argue that acts of worship are not from Islam.
D) To demonstrate that all acts of obedience are automatically called iman.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that all acts of obedience are part of iman because they are from the religion Islam, but cautions that not everything attributed to something shares its name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what ways have you attributed something to your faith—like a good deed or a religious practice—without letting it actually transform you into a person of deep faith? How can you move from 'doing' iman to 'being' iman?
?
The passage warns that not everything attributed to something shares its name. Are there areas in your life where you have mistaken the blessing for the Giver, treating comfort, wealth, or even religious knowledge as if they were the goal themselves? What would change if you saw them only as from Allah, not as Allah?
?
If every act of obedience is only accepted through the lens of Islam, are there places where you have been trying to define goodness on your own terms, outside of what Allah has revealed? What would it cost you to surrender that control completely?

Promise and Threat for Believers
Maturidi cites Quran 33:47 and discusses promise and threat for believers, with a nuance on the fasiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quran 33:47 to show that both promise and threat apply to believers by the absolute name, and that the fasiq is not automatically included in the pure promise.

Core Idea
Believers receive both promise and threat; the fasiq is not guaranteed the pure promise.

Maturidi continues his proof by citing the verse 'And give good tidings to the believers...' (33:47). He notes that similar threats are also addressed to believers by the absolute name 'believers'. This raises the question of whether a fasiq (sinner who commits major sins) is included in that promise. Maturidi explains that the fasiq is not automatically covered by the pure promise, and notes consensus on the generality of promise but disagreement on the generality of threat.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Notice that Maturidi cites a verse about glad tidings and then mentions threats to believers.
The verse 33:47 is about a great bounty for believers.
2
Understand the use of the verse
Maturidi uses this verse to show that the absolute name 'believers' includes both promise and threat in the Quran. He argues that this supports the idea that all acts of obedience are part of iman.
He is linking the discussion back to the definition of iman.
3
Grasp the nuance about the fasiq
Maturidi states that the fasiq is not included in the pure promise given to believers. This means that while the fasiq is still a believer (has iman), he is not guaranteed the same reward without accountability.
This shows the importance of deeds in actualizing the promise.
4
Note the consensus and disagreement
There is consensus that the promise of reward is general for believers, but there is disagreement on whether the threat of punishment is general for all believers. This highlights ongoing theological debates.
This connects to broader issues in kalam about the fate of sinful believers.

Three Answers to the Objection about Verses
Maturidi enumerates three responses to an objection about verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's three complementary explanations of how the divine promise and threat apply to believers.

Core Idea
The promise and threat are eschatological, conditional, and concerned with rights, not faith's core.

Maturidi responds to an objection that used certain verses to limit the divine promise or threat to believers only. He offers three distinct but complementary answers, each explaining a different aspect of how God's promise and threat operate without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the three answers
Read the Arabic passage identifying the three answers. Notice that each is introduced sequentially.
The answers are linked by the phrase 'أجوبة ثلاثة'.
2
Analyze each answer
For each answer, consider its key idea: (1) eschatological outcome, (2) conditional on actualization, (3) punishment is for rights, not faith.
3
Connect to the objection
How does each answer refute the claim that the verses restrict promise or threat exclusively to believers?
Think about the complementary nature of the answers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لما استدل بالآيات أجوبة ثلاثة: أحدها الإخبار عن منتهى الأحوال، وإلى ذلك مرجع كل مؤمن. والثانى أن يكون الوعد للذين حققوا الإيمان بأخلاقه، وما دله عليه، وجائز تسمية أحد بشيء، ذلك اسم لأمر تتصل به أمور عند جميع تلك الأمور، ويجوز عند إقرار الاسم بالذي به سمى، والله أعلم. والثالث أن يكون له ذلك الجزاء، وما يعاقب به، فهو يعاقب بحقوقه، وليس الذى يصيبه بحق دينه مقصر عنه حق دينه. مع ما بين الله أن له فضلا كبيرا؛ ليعلم أن الجزاء للخيرات منه أفضال، وما كان من حكمة الأفضال فإلى من يقوم به ذلك من اختيار أحوال وأوقات. تكلف نصب قول للمرجئة بما يعلم كل متأمل فيه ممن قد عرف مذاهبهم كذبه فيه ليكون له سبيل الطعن عليهم، تركت ذكره لقلة النفع فيه؛ إذ هو كذب. ثم قال : إذ ثبت بمتفق القول على أحد الوعيد الفاسق إن مات قبل أن يتوب بين أهل الإرجاء، وأن يجوز أن يكون هو المعنى به، ويجوز أن لا يكون إلا ما قال مقاتل : إنه من أهل الوعد لا محالة ولا يترك لمثله الإجماع إنه ليس بمؤمن. فيقال له : لو كان بالذي ذكرته يثبت الذي ادعيت، فإذ كان القول عنهم هو خد ما حكيت يفسد ما ادعيت، ثم أكثر المنتسبين إلى الإرجاء ينكر الوعيد أن يكون في غير المستحلين معروف ذلك بينهم، فهو يوضح ما ادعينا عليه من الكذب في الحكاية، والله أعلم.
Then when he used the verses as evidence, [there are] three answers: One of them: the report about the end states, and to that is the return of every believer. The second: that the promise is for those who actualized iman with its character traits and what it indicates. And it is permissible to name someone by something, that name for a matter to which matters are connected according to all those matters, and it is permissible when acknowledging the name by what he is named, and God knows best. The third: that he has that recompense, and whatever he is punished with, he is punished for his rights (huquq), and that which afflicts him by the right of his religion is not falling short of the right of his religion. Along with what God has made clear that He has great bounty, so that it is known that the recompense for good deeds is favors from Him, and whatever is from the wisdom of favors depends on the one who performs that from choosing states and times. He labored to set up a saying for the Murji'ah, by which every reflective person who knows their sects knows his falsehood in it so that he may have a means to attack them; I left its mention because of its lack of benefit—since it is false. Then he said: Since it is established by agreed statement that the threat for the fasiq if he dies before repenting is among the people of Ijja' (Murji'ah), and that it is possible that he is meant by it, and it is possible that it is not, except what Muqatil said: that he is among the people of the promise inevitably, and the consensus that he is not a believer is not left for the likes of him. So it is said to him: If it were by what you mentioned that what you claimed is established, then since the statement from them is a deception of what you reported, it invalidates what you claimed. Then most of those who attribute themselves to Ijja' deny that the threat is for other than those who consider things permissible, which is known among them. So it clarifies what we claimed against him of lying in the report, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 945

Commentary
This passage provides three responses to an argument that used verses to limit the promise or threat to believers only. First answer: the verses report the ultimate outcome, which is the return of every believer—this means the promise is eschatological, not necessarily immediate. Second answer: the promise is for those who have actualized iman with its moral character; it is permissible to name someone by a quality even if the full reality is not yet attained, similar to calling a growing child 'learned' (alim) by anticipation. Third answer: the recompense for good deeds is a favor from God, and punishment for the fasiq is for his shortcomings regarding rights (huquq) not for the core of faith. Then the author dismisses a fabricated saying attributed to the Murji'ah as a lie, implying it was set up to attack them. Finally, he notes that among the Murji'ah, there is consensus that the threat applies specifically to those who consider sins permissible (mustahillun), not to all fasiq. This shows that the opponent's claim about the Murji'ah is false. The refutation uses the Murji'ah's own recognized position to undermine the opponent's argument.

Key Terms
أجوبة
وعد
وعيد
مؤمن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third answer, why is the fasiq punished?
A) Because he lacks faith entirely.
B) For falling short of the rights (huquq) due, not for his faith.
C) Because God punishes arbitrarily.
D) Because the Murji'ah say so.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's three answers collectively show that divine promise and threat are not contradictory but operate on different levels: eschatological, conditional, and concerned with rights and favors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You recite verses of promise and threat regularly. But have you ever actually confronted the possibility that you might not be among those who have 'actualized iman with its character traits'? What one specific character trait do you lack that you know you need to work on starting today?
?
When you make a mistake or sin, do you immediately interpret it as God punishing you for weak faith, or as a consequence of neglecting your own rights and duties? How would your repentance change if you embraced Maturidi's distinction?
?
The lesson says reward is from God's favor, not a right. Do you live as though God owes you paradise for your deeds, or do you live in hopeful gratitude? What would change in your daily worship if you truly believed every good thing is a pure favor?

Refutation of a Fabricated Murji'ite Claim
Maturidi refutes a fabricated Murji'ite saying and clarifies their view on the threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's method of refuting a false saying attributed to the Murji'ah and clarifying their actual position on the threat.

Core Idea
The opponent's claim about Murji'ah is false; they restrict the threat to those who make sin permissible.

After giving three answers, Maturidi addresses a saying fabricated against the Murji'ah. He dismisses it as a lie and then uses the recognized Murji'ah position to refute the opponent's argument, showing that the Murji'ah themselves deny the threat applies to non-mustahillun.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fabricated saying
The passage mentions that someone labored to set up a saying for the Murji'ah, but Maturidi omits it because it is a lie meant to attack them.
2
Understand the Murji'ah's actual position
The text states that most Murji'ah deny the threat except for those who consider sins permissible (mustahillun).
3
See how the refutation works
Maturidi argues that if the opponent's claim were true, it would be contradicted by the Murji'ah's own recognized position, proving the opponent a liar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لما استدل بالآيات أجوبة ثلاثة: أحدها الإخبار عن منتهى الأحوال، وإلى ذلك مرجع كل مؤمن. والثانى أن يكون الوعد للذين حققوا الإيمان بأخلاقه، وما دله عليه، وجائز تسمية أحد بشيء، ذلك اسم لأمر تتصل به أمور عند جميع تلك الأمور، ويجوز عند إقرار الاسم بالذي به سمى، والله أعلم. والثالث أن يكون له ذلك الجزاء، وما يعاقب به، فهو يعاقب بحقوقه، وليس الذى يصيبه بحق دينه مقصر عنه حق دينه. مع ما بين الله أن له فضلا كبيرا؛ ليعلم أن الجزاء للخيرات منه أفضال، وما كان من حكمة الأفضال فإلى من يقوم به ذلك من اختيار أحوال وأوقات. تكلف نصب قول للمرجئة بما يعلم كل متأمل فيه ممن قد عرف مذاهبهم كذبه فيه ليكون له سبيل الطعن عليهم، تركت ذكره لقلة النفع فيه؛ إذ هو كذب. ثم قال : إذ ثبت بمتفق القول على أحد الوعيد الفاسق إن مات قبل أن يتوب بين أهل الإرجاء، وأن يجوز أن يكون هو المعنى به، ويجوز أن لا يكون إلا ما قال مقاتل : إنه من أهل الوعد لا محالة ولا يترك لمثله الإجماع إنه ليس بمؤمن. فيقال له : لو كان بالذي ذكرته يثبت الذي ادعيت، فإذ كان القول عنهم هو خد ما حكيت يفسد ما ادعيت، ثم أكثر المنتسبين إلى الإرجاء ينكر الوعيد أن يكون في غير المستحلين معروف ذلك بينهم، فهو يوضح ما ادعينا عليه من الكذب في الحكاية، والله أعلم.
Then when he used the verses as evidence, [there are] three answers: One of them: the report about the end states, and to that is the return of every believer. The second: that the promise is for those who actualized iman with its character traits and what it indicates. And it is permissible to name someone by something, that name for a matter to which matters are connected according to all those matters, and it is permissible when acknowledging the name by what he is named, and God knows best. The third: that he has that recompense, and whatever he is punished with, he is punished for his rights (huquq), and that which afflicts him by the right of his religion is not falling short of the right of his religion. Along with what God has made clear that He has great bounty, so that it is known that the recompense for good deeds is favors from Him, and whatever is from the wisdom of favors depends on the one who performs that from choosing states and times. He labored to set up a saying for the Murji'ah, by which every reflective person who knows their sects knows his falsehood in it so that he may have a means to attack them; I left its mention because of its lack of benefit—since it is false. Then he said: Since it is established by agreed statement that the threat for the fasiq if he dies before repenting is among the people of Ijja' (Murji'ah), and that it is possible that he is meant by it, and it is possible that it is not, except what Muqatil said: that he is among the people of the promise inevitably, and the consensus that he is not a believer is not left for the likes of him. So it is said to him: If it were by what you mentioned that what you claimed is established, then since the statement from them is a deception of what you reported, it invalidates what you claimed. Then most of those who attribute themselves to Ijja' deny that the threat is for other than those who consider things permissible, which is known among them. So it clarifies what we claimed against him of lying in the report, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 945

Commentary
This passage provides three responses to an argument that used verses to limit the promise or threat to believers only. First answer: the verses report the ultimate outcome, which is the return of every believer—this means the promise is eschatological, not necessarily immediate. Second answer: the promise is for those who have actualized iman with its moral character; it is permissible to name someone by a quality even if the full reality is not yet attained, similar to calling a growing child 'learned' (alim) by anticipation. Third answer: the recompense for good deeds is a favor from God, and punishment for the fasiq is for his shortcomings regarding rights (huquq) not for the core of faith. Then the author dismisses a fabricated saying attributed to the Murji'ah as a lie, implying it was set up to attack them. Finally, he notes that among the Murji'ah, there is consensus that the threat applies specifically to those who consider sins permissible (mustahillun), not to all fasiq. This shows that the opponent's claim about the Murji'ah is false. The refutation uses the Murji'ah's own recognized position to undermine the opponent's argument.

Correction: The passage states that most Murji'ah deny the threat only for those who consider sins permissible, but for non-mustahillun fasiq, the threat is possible.
