Maturidi's Refutation of the Revelational-Only View of Faith's Name
Maturidi refutes the claim that the name of faith is only from revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi argues the name of faith is rooted in language (lugha), not exclusively in revelation, and how this relates to the Khawarij and Murji'ah positions.

Core Idea
Faith's name is a linguistic reality, not a purely revelational construct.

In this lesson, we examine Maturidi's response to an opponent who claimed that the terms 'faith' and 'disbelief' come only from revelation. Maturidi counters that both the Khawarij and Murji'ah actually base their naming on linguistic necessity, though they differ in inference regarding major sins. This passage clarifies the foundational relationship between language and theological naming.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text and the literal English translation. This gives you the author's own words before interpretation.
Pay attention to the flow: opponent's claim, then refutation, then explanation of two groups.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent asserts that the name of faith is only derived from revelation (sam'), not from language or analogy. What evidence does he offer?
He mentions mass transmission and consensus as sources.
3
Notice Maturidi's initial refutation
Maturidi emphatically denies the opponent's report, calling it a lie. He insists that the Khawarij and Murji'ah both use linguistic necessity.
This is a strong rhetorical move.
4
Compare the Khawarij and Murji'ah positions
The Khawarij infer from a major sin that the person lied in their affirmation (tasdiq). The Murji'ah say the inference only concerns the apparent truthfulness, not the reality of tasdiq.
Both still rely on language, not revelation alone.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that both groups base their naming on language, proving the opponent's claim false. Faith is essentially tasdiq (affirmation).
The passage ends with a declaration of the opponent's lie.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج بنفى اسم الكفر والإيمان عن صاحب الكبيرة، إن المرجئة والخوارج اتفقوا على أن اسم الإيمان لا يوجد بالقياس مما أوجبته اللغة، وإنما كان من جهة السمع، فلا تجوز التسمية بواحد إلا بالتواتر بالسمع أو الإجماع، وزعم أن هذه حجة كافية قال الشيخ رحمه الله: نقول وبالله التوفيق: كذب في الحكاية عمن بلغ ذلك، بل هم أجمعوا على تحقيق الاسم بما أوجبته اللغة، لكن الخوارج استدلت بارتكاب الكبيرة على كذبه فيما أظهر من التصديق بقوله: أَحَسِبَ النَّاسُ، والمرجئة زعمت أن الاستدلال لظهور الصدق والكذب فى الغالب لا أن التصديق فى الحقيقة لا يكون دونه، والإيمان هو ذلك التصديق في الحقيقة، لو كان ليس فى الكبيرة نفيه ولا في إبقائها بحقيقة فى حق الوجود وإن كان في حق الدلالة على المستدل، والله أعلم. فحصل إذا القول منهم على ما توجبه اللغة، وظهر كذبه فى الحكاية.
Then he argued with the denial of the name of disbelief and faith from the committer of a major sin, saying that the Murji'ah and Khawarij agreed that the name of faith does not exist by analogy from what language necessitates, but rather it is from the side of revelation (sam'), so it is not permissible to name anyone with it except through a mass-transmitted report or consensus. He claimed that this is a sufficient proof. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: we say, and with God is success: he lied in the narration from whomever reached that. Rather, they all agreed on the realization of the name according to what language necessitates. But the Khawarij inferred from committing the major sin his lying in what he showed of affirmation (tasdiq) by His saying: 'Does man think...' (Quran 29:2). And the Murji'ah claimed that the inference is for the apparent of truthfulness and falsehood in most cases, not that affirmation (tasdiq) in reality is without it. And faith is that affirmation (tasdiq) in reality. It would not be in the major sin its negation nor in its remaining a reality in terms of existence, even if it is in terms of indicating for the one who infers. And God knows best. So the statement from them results according to what language necessitates, and his lie appeared in the narration.
Source pages p. 951

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific claim. The opponent argued that the Murji'ah and Khawarij agreed that the name of faith is only from revelation (sam') and not from language, so naming someone as believer can only be by revelation or consensus. The Shaykh (Maturidi) refutes this as a lie, asserting that both groups actually base their naming on language. However, they differ in inference: the Khawarij infer that committing a major sin proves that the person was lying in his professed affirmation (tasdiq), while the Murji'ah say that the inference is based on the usual appearance of truthfulness or falsehood, not that the reality of tasdiq is nullified by sin. Faith is the reality of tasdiq, which is not negated by sin in terms of existence, even if it indicates something to the inferrer. Thus, both groups rely on language, and the opponent's claim is false.

Key Terms
اسم الإيمان
السمع
اللغة
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The name of faith is solely determined by revelation (sam') and cannot be inferred from language or analogy.
Correction: Maturidi shows that both Khawarij and Murji'ah rely on linguistic necessity (lugha) to determine the name of faith, even though they differ in how they apply it.

Checkpoint
How do the Khawarij and Murji'ah differ in their inference regarding the name of faith?
A) Both agree it is from revelation alone
B) Khawarij infer lying from sin, Murji'ah infer truthfulness from probability
C) Khawarij rely on consensus, Murji'ah on analogy
D) They have no difference

Key Takeaway
Maturidi insists that the name of faith derives from language, not revelation alone, and the Khawarij and Murji'ah confirm this despite their different inferences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, does it shake your conviction that you are a believer? How does Maturidi's point that faith (tasdiq) remains a reality in terms of existence—even if sin indicates something else—challenge you to hold onto your faith without despair?

What concrete step can you take this week to make your faith more linguistically honest—that is, to let your words and deeds match the reality of tasdiq in your heart?

The Two Opinions on the Major Sinner and the Mu'tazilite Error
Maturidi states the two early opinions on the major sinner and criticizes the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two early Islamic positions on the status of a major sinner and how the Mu'tazila deviated from this consensus.

Core Idea
The early community held two nuanced views; the Mu'tazila oversimplified by dropping one.

Before the rise of the Mu'tazila, the early Muslim community debated the status of a person who commits a major sin. Two positions emerged: the sinner is either a sinful believer (mu'min fasiq) or a sinful disbeliever (kafir fasiq). This passage by Imam Maturidi explains these views, uses an analogy to clarify degrees of prohibition and permissibility, and then criticizes the Mu'tazila for discarding one of the names, thereby opposing the community's consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get a sense of the author's argument.
Pay attention to the terms 'mu'min fasiq' and 'kafir fasiq'.
2
Identify the two opinions
The passage states that the early community had two opinions on the major sinner. Note what they are and how they are described.
3
Understand the analogy
Maturidi uses an analogy of 'forbidden disliked' and 'permissible disliked' to explain that rulings can have degrees. Reflect on how this relates to the two opinions.
4
Identify the Mu'tazilite error
The passage criticizes the Mu'tazila for dropping one of the two names. Determine which name was dropped and why this is problematic.
The dropped name is the one indicating contention (the 'fasiq' part?).
5
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider how this early debate and the Mu'tazilite deviation shaped later theological discussions on faith and sin.
This lesson guards against the misconception of a single early view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأمة كانت قبل حدوث الاعتزال وأهله على قولين في صاحب الكبيرة على أنه مؤمن فاسق أو كافر فاسق؛ ليعلم وجه كفره عند من یراه كافرا؛ ووجه فسقه عند من يراه فاسقا، وذلك ما يقال: حرام مكروه وحلال مكروه؛ ليعلم أن الحرمة هى بيان أخف الحرمتين وهي حرمة الكرامة لا حرمة الإطلاق، وإن الحل ليس هو حل الإيثار والرغبة بل فيه بعض ما يورث الشبه، فمثله أمر الأمة فيما ذكرت. ثم اسقطت المعتزلة أحد الأسمين وهو الذي يعرف معنى التنازع، وألزم الآخر على الاتفاق في منع ذلك مطلقا دون معرفة حقيقته، فخالفوا بذلك الأمة.. وهذه حجة مقنعة لمن تصح [نيته] لله، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the community before the occurrence of the Mu'tazila and its people had two opinions concerning the committer of a major sin: that he is a sinful believer (mu'min fasiq) or a sinful disbeliever (kafir fasiq); so that the face of his disbelief is known to one who sees him as a disbeliever, and the face of his sinfulness is known to one who sees him as sinful. And that is what is said: forbidden disliked and permissible disliked; so that it is known that the prohibition is an expression of the lighter of the two prohibitions, which is the prohibition of honor (karama), not the absolute prohibition. And the permissibility is not permissibility of preference and desire but rather there is in it something that causes ambiguity. Similarly is the affair of the community regarding what I mentioned. Then the Mu'tazila dropped one of the two names—which is the one that indicates the meaning of contention—and imposed the other absolutely, without knowing its reality. Thus they opposed the community. And this is a convincing proof for one whose intention is sincere for God, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 952

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation. The author states that before the Mu'tazila emerged, the early community had two opinions on the committer of a major sin: either he is a sinful believer (mu'min fasiq) or a sinful disbeliever (kafir fasiq). This shows the reasoning behind each view. He analogizes with terms like 'forbidden disliked' and 'permissible disliked' to explain that rulings can have degrees. The Mu'tazila then dropped one of these names, specifically the one that indicates contention, and insisted on the other absolutely, without understanding its true meaning. This opposes the consensus of the early community. The author concludes that this is a convincing proof for those with sincere intention.

Key Terms
صاحب الكبيرة (committer of major sin)
مؤمن فاسق (sinful believer)
كافر فاسق (sinful disbeliever)
المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the early Muslim community had a single, unanimous view on the status of a major sinner.
Correction: In fact, there were two distinct opinions: one considering the sinner a sinful believer (mu'min fasiq), and the other a sinful disbeliever (kafir fasiq).

Checkpoint
What were the two opinions of the early community regarding the committer of a major sin?
A) Repentant or unrepentant
B) Mu'min fasiq or kafir fasiq
C) Mu'min or munafiq
D) Fasiq or murtadd

Key Takeaway
The early community held two nuanced views on the major sinner, and the Mu'tazila erred by discarding one of them, showing the importance of preserving theological nuance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been quick to label someone who committed a major sin as either a complete believer or a complete disbeliever, ignoring the nuanced position of the early community? What would it cost you to hold the tension of two true statements at once?
?
The Mu'tazila dropped a name because it caused contention. In your own life, do you avoid holding onto truths that create discomfort or ambiguity? Ask yourself honestly: would you rather have a simple but flawed answer or a complex but accurate one?
?
The author says this proof is convincing for one whose intention is sincere. Is your intention in seeking theological knowledge truly for God, or do you seek confirmation of your own opinions? How can you purify your intention today, before reading further?

The Objection: Why Do Names Differ from Rulings?
Maturidi presents an objection and its initial rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers the objection that names should determine rulings to be a deviation.

Core Idea
Names of sins do not erase the name of faith.

Maturidi anticipates an objection from those who think that if we name actions, we should name the doer accordingly. If a thief is called a thief, why not call a sinning believer an unbeliever? He shows this reasoning misses the nature of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage where Maturidi presents the objection against his own position.
Notice the repetition of 'الأحكام'.
2
Identify the comparison
The objector compares naming a thief to naming a believer who sins. Why the difference?
3
See the rebuttal
Maturidi says this is a clear deviation. Understand why he says that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بأنك اتبعت الاسم الأحكام الأحكام. هلا فرقت بين أصحاب الكبائر بما اختلفت أحكامها مهما قال: أفعل، فأسمى هذا سارقا وهذا فيقال: فإذ سميته فاسقا تحقيق اسمه الذى به فسق ولم تمنع عنه مع فعله، مما بالك منعت عن المؤمن اسم الإيمان الذي هو له مستحق لفعله، إلا أن توسع التسمية بما يقبح من الفعل ويمنع بما يحسن وذلك جور فى الفعل. لم يجب بجلد الثمانين ولكن بالموالاة والتعظيم، وهذا بين الحيد عن تقدير المعارض له، وإنما أراد والله أعلم، أن الأسماء لم تقدر عن الأحكام بوجود الاختلاف في الأحكام على الاستواء فى الأسماء، وبذلك كان تقديره في الابتداء. ثم اسم الفسق ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم الموالاة والتعظيم متفاوت على تفاضل المنازل والدرجات في المنازل في الدين كالرسل ثم الأئمة ثم العلماء ثم المؤمنين، وعلى ذلك لازم الأمران جميعا في الدين، ارتكبوا الكبائر على قدر ما أوتوا من الحسنات، والمقت بما أوتوا من السيئات.
Then he objected to himself: you followed the name [in] the rulings, the rulings. Why did you not differentiate among the committers of major sins according to the differences of their rulings? Whenever he said: I did such-and-such, so I name this one a thief and this one, so it is said: since you name him sinful (fasiq) as a realization of the name by which he sinned, and you do not withhold from him [the name] with his action, how is it that you withhold from the believer the name of faith which is deserved for his action? Unless you broaden the naming for ugly action and withhold for good action, and that is injustice in action. It does not require flogging of eighty [lashes] but rather [requires] love and veneration. And this is a clear deviation from the estimation of the objector. And he intended, and God knows best, that the names are not estimated by the rulings due to the presence of difference in rulings while being equal in names, and that was his estimation initially. Then the name of sinfulness (fisq), and there is no power except with God. Then love and veneration vary according to the differentiation of ranks and degrees in the ranks in religion, like the messengers, then the imams, then the scholars, then the believers. And on that basis, both matters are necessary in the religion: they committed major sins to the extent of the good deeds they were given, and are hated to the extent of the evil deeds they were given.
Source pages p. 952

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that if the name determines the ruling, then why don't we differentiate among major sinners? For example, we call a thief by his action, but we call a fornicator likewise. If we can call a person sinful (fasiq) based on action without withholding the name, then why do we withhold the name of faith from the believer due to his action of sin, unless we arbitrarily broaden naming for ugly actions and narrow for good ones? The author answers that this reasoning is flawed: the name of faith is not based on the same kind of action as names of sins. Faith is a comprehensive reality (tasdiq) that is not negated by sin. The objection would require applying the same standard, which would lead to injustice. He then explains that love and veneration differ according to religious ranks, and major sins affect the balance of good and evil deeds, but the name of faith remains.

Key Terms
اسم الفسق (name of sin)
اسم الإيمان (name of faith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a believer who commits a major sin should no longer be called a believer.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this reasoning is flawed; the name of faith is not based on the same type of action as names of sins.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why should the name of faith be withheld from a sinning believer?
A) Because he committed a sin
B) Because the name of faith is like any other name based on action
C) Because he is a hypocrite
D) Because he deserves punishment

Key Takeaway
The objection that faith should be negated by major sin is based on a misunderstanding of how the name of faith relates to action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim commit a major sin, do you subconsciously question their faith? How does Maturidi’s argument force you to reconsider that response?
?
Have you ever felt that your own sin makes you unworthy of being called a believer? How does this passage protect you from that despair while still holding you accountable?
?
If you truly internalized that the name of faith is not revoked by sin, how would your attitude toward your own repentance and toward others' mistakes change starting today?

Love and Veneration by Rank in Religion
Maturidi explains the basis of love, veneration, and the weighing of sins against good deeds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that love and veneration among believers vary according to their ranks, and that major sins are weighed against good deeds.

Core Idea
Love and veneration depend on rank, and sins are weighed against good deeds.

Continuing his response, Maturidi explains that the names of actions do not uniformly determine one's status. Instead, love and veneration are proportional to one's rank in religion. Even prophets committed minor faults, but their rank is high. The overall balance of good and evil deeds determines one's standing, not any single sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the continuation
Read the Arabic from 'وإنما أراد' to the end. This is the core of Maturidi's positive argument.
2
Identify the ranking
Note the hierarchy: messengers, imams, scholars, believers. This shows different degrees of love and veneration.
3
Understand the weighing
Maturidi says both good and bad deeds are taken into account. Major sins affect but do not remove the name of faith.
This refutes the objection.

Maturidi's Critique of the Mu'tazila on Naming
Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila for dropping one name and imposing the other absolutely.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's criticism of the Mu'tazila for abandoning the nuanced naming of the committer of a major sin, and why he considers his argument convincing.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila lost the truth by dropping one name and absolutising the other.

Imam Maturidi identifies a crucial error in the Mu'tazila's approach to naming the person who commits a major sin. They abandoned the tension captured by the two names used by the Ummah, settling for a single absolute label. In this passage, Maturidi declares this a compelling proof for those sincere in seeking truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two names
Note that the Ummah used two names for the grave sinner (e.g., 'mu'min' and 'fasiq' or similar). Maturidi highlights that the Mu'tazila dropped one of these names.
The dropped name is the one that indicates 'tanazu'' (contention) between faith and sin.
2
Understand 'dropping one name'
The Mu'tazila ceased using one of the names, thereby ignoring the nuance that both names together capture the individual's mixed state of faith and sin.
3
Recognize the consequence
By imposing the other name absolutely, they contradicted the practice of the Ummah, which maintained both names to reflect the complexity.
This contradiction is a key part of Maturidi's critique.
4
Reflect on the proof
Maturidi states that this argument is convincing for one whose intention is sincere for Allah. Ask yourself why this would be a strong proof against the Mu'tazila.
The proof relies on the Ummah's consensus and the internal coherence of naming.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اسقطت المعتزلة أحد الأسمين وهو الذي يعرف معنى التنازع، وألزم الآخر على الاتفاق في منع ذلك مطلقا دون معرفة حقيقته، فخالفوا بذلك الأمة. وهذه حجة مقنعة لمن تصح نيته لله، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the Mu'tazila dropped one of the two names, which is the one that indicates the meaning of contention (tanazu'), and imposed the other absolutely, without knowing its reality, and thus contradicted the Ummah. And this is a convincing proof for one whose intention is sincere for Allah, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 952

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Mu'tazila's approach to naming the committer of a major sin. Maturidi states that the Mu'tazila dropped one of the two names (presumably 'fasiq' or 'mu'min'?) that the Ummah used – specifically the name that indicates the meaning of 'tanazu'' (contention, i.e., the conflict between faith and sin). By dropping that name, they ignored the nuanced tension. They then imposed the other name absolutely, without understanding its true reality, thereby contradicting the consensus (or practice) of the Ummah. Maturidi declares this argument convincing for one with sincere intention for Allah.

Key Terms

اسمين
تنازع
أمة
حجة مقنعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila merely had a different interpretation of the same names used by the Ummah.
Correction: Maturidi asserts that they actually dropped one of the two names entirely, contradicting the Ummah's practice.

Checkpoint

D) They accepted the Ummah's two-name tradition without change

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila erred by discarding the nuanced dual naming of the grave sinner, contradicting the Ummah's practice, and Maturidi considers this a clear proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Would you be willing to submit your private verdict to the community's practice even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
Maturidi ends by saying 'there is no power except with Allah.' Does your theological certainty—or even your doubts—drive you to that same humble reliance, or do you lean on your own ability to sort truth from error? The Objection and Answer: Names, Rulings, and Inconsistency
Maturidi presents a self-objection and its refutation regarding names and rulers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's response to the objection that naming believers and sinners inconsistently follows names rather than rulings.

Core Idea
Names follow acts, but rulings can differ without changing names.

Maturidi anticipates an objection to his earlier reasoning: why differentiate between sinners when rulings vary, while naming believers inconsistently? He presents the objection and then refutes it by clarifying that names are not determined by the variety of rulings, but by the acts themselves. This lesson unpacks that dense exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's challenge carefully. Note the claim that Maturidi follows the name (اسم) rather than the rulings (أحكام). The objector argues that one should differentiate between sinners based on their differing rulings.
The objector points to the example of calling one a thief and another a sinner.
2
Grasp Maturidi's response
Maturidi refutes by saying that the proper response is not a fixed legal punishment (like lashing for false accusation) but rather graded friendship (موالاة) and glorification (تعظيم). This shows that the opponent's estimation is deviated.
The response is about maintaining the name of faith while differentiating degrees of relationship.
3
Connect to core doctrine
Reflect on how this clarifies that the name 'faith' is not removed by sin, but relationships are graded. The key insight: names are not determined by the existence of differing rulings; names remain constant across acts.
Maturidi's conclusion: 'names are not determined by rulings by the existence of difference in rulings while names are equal.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بأنك اتبعت الاسم الأحكام. هلا فرقت بين أصحاب الكبائر بما اختلفت أحكامها مهما قال: أفعل، فأسمى هذا سارقا وهذا فيقال: فإذ سميته فاسقا تحقيق اسمه الذي به فسق ولم تمنع عنه مع فعله، مما بالك منعت عن المؤمن اسم الإيمان الذي هو له مستحق لفعله، إلا أن توسع التسمية بما يقبح من الفعل ويمنع بما يحسن وذلك جور فى الفعل. لم يجب بجلد الثمانين ولكن بالموالاة والتعظيم، وهذا بين الحيد عن تقدير المعارض له، وإنما أراد والله أعلم، أن الأسماء لم تقدر عن الأحكام بوجود الاختلاف في الأحكام على الاستواء فى الأسماء، وبذلك كان تقديره في الابتداء ثم اسم الفسق ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he objected to himself: 'You followed the name, the rulings.' Why did you not differentiate between the committers of major sins according to the differences in their rulings, whenever he says 'I do'? So I name this one a thief and this one—it is said: So when you named him a sinner (fasiq) as a realization of his name by which he sinned, and you did not withhold from him along with his act, how then do you withhold from the believer the name of faith (iman) which is due to him for his act? Unless you extend naming to what is ugly of the act and withhold from what is good, and that is injustice in action. It is not answered by the lashing of eighty but by friendship (muwalat) and glorification (ta'zim). And this is a clear deviation from the estimation of his opponent. And what he intended, and Allah knows best, is that names are not determined by rulings by the existence of difference in rulings while names are equal. And thus was his estimation initially, then the name of sin (fisq). And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 952

Commentary
This passage is a self-objection followed by its answer. The objector says: 'You follow the name, the rulings' – meaning that you inconsistently apply names based on acts. You should differentiate between sinners based on their rulings. When someone says 'I do' (commit a sin), you call one a thief and another a sinner; but then you withhold the name of 'faith' from a believer even though he has acts of faith. This is an inconsistency unless you arbitrarily extend naming to ugly acts and withhold it from good ones, which is injustice. The answer is that the response is not lashing of eighty (like for false accusation) but rather friendship (muwalat) and glorification (ta'zim) – which are graded. This shows a deviation from the opponent's estimation. The true point: names are not determined by rulings; rulings can differ while names remain the same. So the initial estimation was that the name 'fisq' (sin) applies, but that does not remove the name of faith.

Key Terms
اسم
أحكام
كبيرة
سارق
فاسق
إيمان
موالاة
تعظيم
فسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is about inconsistency in applying names to sinners vs believers.
Correction: The actual issue is the logical relation between names and the acts that deserve them. The objection conflates the diversity of legal rulings with the nature of names.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's response, what is the proper reply to the objection about naming sinners differently?
A) Lashing of eighty
B) Withholding friendship and glorification
C) Applying friendship and glorification in a graded manner
D) Removing the name of faith entirely

Key Takeaway
The name of faith is not negated by sin; believers are differentiated by degrees of friendship and glorification, not by removal of the name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a believer committing a sin, do you mentally strip them of the name 'faith' ('īmān') in your heart? Maturidi's argument says no. How can you actively treat them as a believer while still acknowledging their sin? What specific change in your attitude does this demand?
?
The objector accused Maturidi of inconsistency. Where in your own life are you inconsistent—applying one standard to yourself and another to others when it comes to naming someone as a 'true believer'? Will you commit to examining your heart for such double standards today?
?
Maturidi says the proper response to a sinning believer is not punishment (like lashing) but graded friendship and glorification. Think of a specific person you have been harsh with. How could you replace condemnation with muwalat and ta'zim? What concrete action can you take this week?

The Gradation of Friendship and Glorification in Religion
Maturidi explains that friendship and glorification are graded by religious rank and relates this to the evaluation of sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how friendship (muwalat) and glorification (ta'zim) are graded according to one's rank in religion and how major sins are evaluated in light of good deeds.

Core Idea
Friendship and glorification vary by spiritual rank, and sins are weighed against good deeds.

Maturidi presents a nuanced view of human relationships with Allah and the believers. He teaches that the love and honor we owe to others are not all the same; they depend on one's station in faith. Moreover, when believers commit major sins, these are considered in proportion to the good deeds they have earned. This lesson explores these gradations and how they affect divine judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi lists the ranks: messengers, imams, scholars, believers.
The number 21 may be a verse reference.
2
Understand the gradation of friendship and glorification
Identify that muwalat and ta'zim are not uniform but differ according to the superiority of stations and degrees in religion.
3
Examine the consequence of major sins
Note that Maturidi says major sins are committed according to the measure of good deeds, and abhorrence corresponds to evil deeds. Then consider his ruling on repelling good deeds with non-opposite sins.
4
Reflect on the justice of God
Consider why it is unjust to nullify good deeds with sins that are not direct opposites. This teaches balance in divine judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الموالاة والتعظيم متفاوت على تفاضل المنازل والدرجات في المنازل في الدين كالرسل ثم الأئمة ثم العلماء ثم المؤمنين، وعلى ذلك لازم الأمران جميعا في الدين، ۲۱ ارتكبوا الكبائر على قدر ما أوتوا من الحسنات، والمقت بما أوتوا من السيئات، فمن الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي رام دفع ما جاء به من الخيرات بسيئات لسن بأضداد لهن فهو جائر فى الحكم، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then friendship (muwalat) and glorification (ta'zim) are differentiated according to the superiority of stations and degrees in the stations in religion, like the messengers, then the imams, then the scholars, then the believers. And upon that, both matters are necessary in religion. 21 They committed major sins according to the measure of the good deeds they were given, and abhorrence (maqt) according to the evil deeds they were given. So whoever—Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi—seeks to repel the good deeds he has brought with evil deeds that are not opposites to them, he is unjust in judgment. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 952

Commentary
This passage explains that the concepts of friendship (muwalat) and glorification (ta'zim) are not uniform but vary by rank in the religion: highest for messengers, then imams, scholars, and believers. Therefore, both friendship and the necessary consequences (punishment or abhorrence) apply together in the religion. The text then states that 'they committed major sins according to the measure of the good deeds they were given' – meaning the degree of sin is considered in light of their good deeds. Abhorrence (maqt) corresponds to evil deeds. Then Maturidi comments: whoever tries to repel the good deeds he has brought with evil deeds that are not opposites to them is unjust. In other words, one cannot simply nullify good deeds with sins that are not direct opposites; the judgment must be balanced.

تعظيم
منازل
درجات
رسل
أئمة
علماء
مؤمنين
حسنات
سيئات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that committing a major sin completely removes all friendship and glorification from a believer.
Correction: Maturidi shows that friendship and glorification are graded; a sinner still has some level of wala' based on their good deeds.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how should we understand the relationship between good deeds and major sins?
A) Good deeds are completely nullified by major sins
B) Major sins are committed in proportion to the good deeds one has, and judgment must be balanced
C) Good deeds and sins are unrelated
D) Major sins automatically remove all good deeds

Key Takeaway
The degree of friendship and glorification depends on one's rank in faith, and major sins are judged in balance with good deeds, not by nullifying them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, do you tend to measure yourself by your good deeds or your sins? How does the balanced view from this passage challenge your self-assessment? Will you commit to reviewing your deeds with this balance today?
?
Think of someone you have difficulty loving or respecting because of their sins. Does this passage change how you view them, considering the gradation of friendship based on rank? Are you willing to adjust your attitude toward them based on their faith rather than their faults?
?
You know that you have good deeds, but also sins that are not opposites to those good deeds. Have you ever felt that your sins cancel out your good works? How does Maturidi's warning against such injustice make you reconsider your hope in Allah's mercy? What step will you take this week to hold on to your good deeds without despair?

The Name of Faith Remains Despite Major Sins: The Repentance Verse Proof
Maturidi uses the repentance verse to refute the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the repentance verse to prove that the name 'believer' remains even for those who commit major sins.

Core Idea
Repentance is commanded to believers, not to disbelievers—so sinners are still believers.

The Mu'tazila argued that committing major sins removes one from faith entirely. Maturidi turns to the Quran itself, where Allah addresses sinners as 'O you who have believed' and commands them to repent. This simple fact demolishes the Mu'tazila's claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and the verse
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that the verse begins by addressing the 'believers' and commands them to repent.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila's own rule
Recall that the Mu'tazila claim minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins. So the command to repent must be for major sins.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since the command is for major sinners and they are still called believers, the name of faith remains with them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي ينقض على المعتزلة ابتداء الآية وهو قوله: يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا تُوبُوا إِلَى اللهِ تَوْبَةً نَصُوحًا، ألزمهم التوبة وجعلها شرطا للمغفرة، على ابقاء اسم الإيمان لهم، والصغائر مغفورة باجتناب الكبائر على قولهم، ثبت أن الآية في أصحاب الكبائر، وقد بقي لهم اسم الايمان، ولا قوة إلا بالله. والدليل على أن اسم الإيمان لا يزول بزوال العدالة قوله تعالى: يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا شَهَادَةُ بَيْنَكُمْ إِذَا حَضَرَ أَحَدَكُمُ المَوْتَ حِينَ الوَصِيَّةِ اثْنَانِ ذَوَا عَدْلٍ مِنْكُم، ولو كان كل مؤمن عدلا لكان يقول: اثنان منكم؛ إذ كان ابتداء الآية فى مخاطبة المؤمنين ثبت أن قد يكون مؤمنا عدلا وغير عدل.
Then what refutes the Mu'tazila is the beginning of the verse, which is His saying: 'O you who have believed, repent to Allah with sincere repentance.' He obligated them to repent and made it a condition for forgiveness, while preserving the name of faith for them. And minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins according to their claim. So it is established that the verse is about the committers of major sins, and the name of faith remained for them. And there is no power except with Allah. And the evidence that the name of faith does not disappear with the loss of justice ('adala) is His saying, the Exalted: 'O you who have believed, when death approaches one of you, let two just persons among you be witnesses to the will.' If every believer were just, He would have said 'two from among you'; since the verse begins by addressing believers, it is established that there may be a believer who is just and one who is not just.
Source pages p. 953

Commentary
This passage provides two refutations of the Mu'tazila. First, Maturidi points to the beginning of the verse 'Ya ayyuha alladhina amanu tubu ila Allahi tawbatan nasuhan' (O you who have believed, repent to Allah with sincere repentance). He notes that the verse addresses them as 'those who have believed' (preserving the name of faith) and commands them to repent, making repentance a condition for forgiveness. This shows that the verse is about the committers of major sins, because minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins according to the Mu'tazila's own claim, so the command to repent must be for major sins. Yet they are still addressed as believers. Second, he cites the verse about witnesses for a will: 'O you who have believed, let two just persons among you be witnesses.' The verse distinguishes between believers and just persons; otherwise, 'two from among you' would suffice. Therefore, there can be believers who are not just. This proves that the name of faith does not disappear with the loss of justice.

Key Terms
آية
توبة
مغفرة
صغائر
كبائر
اسم الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila claimed that committing major sins removes the name 'believer' from a person.
Correction: This passage shows that the Quran addresses major sinners as 'believers' and commands them to repent, proving the name remains.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, why does the repentance verse prove that the name 'iman' remains for major sinners?
A) Because the verse commands repentance to all people
B) Because the verse addresses them as 'believers' and the command to repent is for major sins
C) Because minor sins are never forgiven
D) Because the Mu'tazila accept the verse as metaphorical

Key Takeaway
The repentance verse proves that major sinners remain believers because they are commanded to repent as believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that Allah still calls you 'believer' even if you have committed a major sin. If you have fallen into a sin you are ashamed of, does this verse give you hope to turn back to Allah? What is one step you can take today to repent sincerely?
?
The Mu'tazila refused to call sinners believers, but Allah does. Do you ever look down on others who struggle with sin, thinking they have lost their faith? How does this verse challenge you to see them as fellow believers rather than outsiders?
?
Allah commands repentance as a condition for forgiveness. When was the last time you made a sincere, transformative tawbah? If it has been a while, what is holding you back from falling at Allah's feet right now?

Believers Can Be Unjust: The Witness Verse Proof
Maturidi uses the will-witness verse to refute the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse about witnesses for a will proves that believers can be unjust without losing the name of faith.

Core Idea
If every believer were just, the Quran would not need to specify 'two just persons' among believers.

The Mu'tazila equated faith with perfect justice, so a sinner cannot be called a believer. Maturidi responds with a second Quranic proof: the will-witness verse, which distinguishes between being a believer and being just.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and the verse
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the verse addresses believers but then specifies 'two just persons' as witnesses.
2
Analyze the implication
If every believer were just, the adjective 'just' would be redundant. The Quran's wording implies that not all believers are just.
3
Understand the conclusion
Therefore, a believer may be unjust (i.e., commit sins) while still being a believer. The name of faith does not disappear with loss of justice.

Source Text

Major Sinners Remain Believers: Maturidi's Argument from Surah at-Tahrim
Maturidi argues from Quran 66:8 that believers who repent remain believers despite major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses Quran 66:8 to argue that those who commit major sins are still believers and are not eternally punished.

Core Idea
Major sins do not remove the name of faith; believers remain believers even when they sin.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi engages with the Mu'tazilite claim that a believer who commits a major sin would be punished and thus cannot be called a believer. He turns to the Quranic verse (66:8) to show that the title 'believers' is retained for those who repent from major sins, and that punishment, if any, is not eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi begins with the Mu'tazilite objection and then responds.
The passage uses dense theological language; take your time.
2
Identify the three aspects
Maturidi offers three interpretations of 'lā yukhzī'. List them and note how each preserves the possibility of punishment without eternal disgrace.
The third aspect includes examples from other verses about varying punishments.
3
Examine the refutation
Look at the beginning of the verse 'O you who have believed...' How does Maturidi use this to argue that the addressees are believers despite major sins?
He says the verse commands repentance and makes it a condition for forgiveness while preserving the name of faith.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the verse is about committers of major sins and they retain the name of faith. How does this refute the Mu'tazilite position?
This supports the Ahl al-Sunna view that major sins do not nullify iman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج بالآية التي فيها يَوْمَ لَا يَخْزِي اللَّهُ النَّبِيَّ وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا مَعَهُ في صاحب الكبيرة إنه لو كان مؤمنا لكان لا يعذب ولا يوعد عليه. والآية إلى وجوه: أحدها أن لا يجزيه عن شفاعة رسول الله، بل يشفعه فيه وينجيه بها، والثاني أن يكون ذلك عندما يقول لهم: تواهنوا مظالمكم وعلى معذرتكم، والثالث أن يكون لا يجزيهم خزي الكفرة من الخلود في النار؛ إذ هو أنواع كما قال الله تعالى: لَيْسَ لَهُمْ طَعَامٌ إِلَّا مِنْ ضَرَيْع، وقال في موضع: فَلَيْسَ لَهُ الْيَوْمَ هَهُنَا حَمِيمٌ، وَلَا طَعَامُ إِلَّا مِنْ غِسْلِين على اختلاف الأوقات، ويحتمل لا يجزى أي لا يفضح فيهنك ستره، كذلك في كل مؤمن. ثم الذي ينقض على المعتزلة ابتداء الآية وهو قوله: يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا تُوبُوا إِلَى اللهِ تَوْبَةً نَصُوحًا، ألزمهم التوبة وجعلها شرطا للمغفرة، على إبقاء اسم الإيمان لهم، والصغائر مغفورة باجتناب الكبائر على قولهم، ثبت أن الآية في أصحاب الكبائر، وقد بقي لهم اسم الإيمان، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he argued with the verse in which is 'On the Day when Allah will not disgrace the Prophet and those who believed with him' concerning the committer of a major sin: if he were a believer, he would not be punished nor threatened. The verse has aspects: one of them is that He will not suffice him concerning the intercession of the Messenger of Allah, but rather He will intercede him in it and save him by it; second, that that is when He says to them: 'Repudiate your injustices and act upon your excuse'; third, that He will not disgrace them with the disgrace of the disbelievers of eternity in the Fire, since it is of kinds as Allah Almighty said: 'They have no food except from darī‘', and He said in another place: 'So he has no friend here today, nor food except from ghislin' at different times, and it is possible that 'lā yukhzī' means He will not expose his veil, similarly for every believer. Then that which refutes the Mu‘tazila is the beginning of the verse, which is His saying: 'O you who have believed, repent to Allah with sincere repentance.' He obligated them to repent and made it a condition for forgiveness, while preserving the name of faith for them; and minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins according to their statement. It is established that the verse is about the committers of major sins, and the name of faith remains for them. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 953

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the Quranic verse 66:8 to defend the position that a Muslim who commits a major sin (kabīra) remains a believer and is not doomed to eternal punishment. Maturidi begins by citing the part of the verse that promises that Allah will not disgrace the Prophet and those who believed with him on the Day of Judgment. The Mu‘tazilites had argued that if a person guilty of a major sin were truly a believer, he would not be punished or threatened with punishment. Maturidi responds by offering three possible interpretations (wujūh) of the phrase 'lā yukhzī' (will not disgrace). First, it means that Allah will not refuse the Prophet's intercession for such a sinner, but rather will accept his intercession and save him. Second, it could mean that at the time of judgment, Allah will tell them to settle their injustices and present their excuse (i.e., they will be held accountable but ultimately saved). Third, it could mean that they will not be disgraced with the same kind of disgrace as the disbelievers who are eternally in the Fire; rather, their punishment, if any, will be temporary, because punishment is of various degrees, as indicated by verses describing different types of punishment for different groups. Maturidi then strengthens his argument by pointing to the beginning of the same verse, 'O you who have believed, repent to Allah with sincere repentance.' He argues that the verse addresses the believers (those who have the name of iman) and commands them to repent, and makes repentance a condition for forgiveness. This would be meaningless if the addressees were not believers; but they are addressed as believers, so they retain the name of faith even while committing major sins. Moreover, the Mu‘tazilite doctrine states that minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins; so the verse's command to repent from major sins implies that major sins do not remove iman. Thus, the verse proves that the committers of major sins still have the name of faith.

Key Terms
الكبيرة
الإيمان
التوبة
الشفاعة
الخزي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'لا يخزي' means no punishment at all for believers who commit major sins.
Correction: Maturidi explains that it refers to the type of disgrace (not eternal like disbelievers), not total exemption from punishment.

Checkpoint
According to the third interpretation in this passage, what does 'لَا يَخْزِي' mean for the believers who committed major sins?
A) They will not be punished at all
B) They will not be disgraced like the disbelievers who are eternally in the Fire
C) They will be immediately forgiven without repentance
D) They will be deprived of the Prophet's intercession

Key Takeaway
Major sins do not negate faith; believers who repent from major sins remain believers and are not eternally punished.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The verse commands you, as a believer, to repent sincerely. Is there a major sin you have been rationalizing, assuming your faith is already ruined? What step will you take today to turn back to Allah, trusting that He still calls you a believer?
?
Maturidi explains that 'no disgrace' for believers means they will not be eternally shamed like disbelievers. Do you live with the weight of past sins, fearing that Allah has abandoned you? How does this truth free you to repent and hope in His mercy, even tonight?
?
Allah preserved the name 'believer' for you even when you sin. Does this comfort you or make you complacent? What will you do to ensure this mercy leads to genuine change rather than excuses?

Faith Does Not Require Justice: Quranic Evidence
Maturidi presents Quranic and consensus-based proof that faith remains despite loss of justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic verses and community consensus to argue that faith (īmān) remains even when a believer commits major sins and loses justice (ʿadāla).

Core Idea
Faith is not lost through sin; justice is a separate quality.

Maturidi now turns to the Quran and the practice of the Muslim community to refute the Muʿtazilite claim that major sins remove a person from faith. He presents a series of verses that require justice in witnesses, but the very condition of justice shows that believers can be unjust. The community's consensus on examining witnesses further proves that faith does not depend on moral perfection. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the translation to understand the meaning.
Notice how Maturidi cites multiple verses in succession.
2
Identify the key verses and their implications
List the verses Maturidi quotes: 5:106, 2:282, 65:2, 4:6. For each, ask: Why does the condition prove that not all believers are just?
The condition of justice would be redundant if all believers were just.
3
Understand the logical inference and consensus
Maturidi concludes that if faith required justice, there would be no need to examine witnesses. The community's practice shows otherwise. Reflect on how this consensus refutes the Muʿtazilites.
The keyword here is 'إجماع' (consensus).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أن اسم الإيمان لا يزول بزوال العدالة قوله تعالى: يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا شَهَادَةُ بَيْنَكُمْ إِذَا حَضَرَ أَحَدَكُمُ المَوْتَ حِينَ الوَصِيَّةِ اثْنَانِ ذَوَا عَدْلٍ مِنْكُم، ولو كان كل مؤمن عدلا لكان يقول: اثنان منكم؛ إذ كان ابتداء الآية في مخاطبة المؤمنين ثبت أن قد يكون مؤمنا عدلا وغير عدل. وقال الله تعالى: يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِذَا تَدَايَنْتُمْ بِدَيْنٍ إلى قوله: مِمَّنْ تَرْضَوْنَ مِنَ الشُّهَدَاءِ، فلو كان كل مؤمن مرضيا لم يكن للشرط فائدة. وكذلك قوله: وَأَشْهِدُوا ذَوِي عَدْلٍ مِنْكُمْ ثبت أن المؤمن يكون عدلا وغير عدل، وكذلك قوله: فَإِنْ آنَسْتُمْ مِنْهُمْ رُشْدًا فَادْفَعُوا إِلَيْهِمْ أَمْوَالَهُمْ، ثبت أنه قد يكون منهم رشيد وغير رشيد. ولو كان كل مؤمن عدلا وكل من ليس بعدل ليس بمؤمن لكان لا شهادة ترد بالفسق بعد الامتحان ولا يجوز السؤال عن الأحوال ليعلم بها العدالة والفسق، بل على المكان الذي يسأل عما عليه من الإيمان في ذلك ويمكنه الوفاء، فيجب قبول شهادته بلا سؤال عنه ولا اعتبار بأحواله: وفى إجماع الأمة على الفحص عن الأحوال وترك النزول على ما يظهر من الأموال التي يكتفى بها فيما كان شرائطها الإيمان من الحل والحرمة والتوارث، ثم العبادات دليل يبين أن الإيمان وما به يصير المرء مؤمنا ويستوجب أحكامه ليس هو كل ما يبقى أنواع الفسوق والعصيان، ولا قوة إلا بالله وعلى ذلك معنى أمر هذه الأمة في تعاهد الصلوات في الجماعات والصيام وإخراج الزكوات على ما عليه هم من الاختلاف في هتك الحرمات والانهماك في المعاصي، ثبت بالذي عليه الأمة عدول المعتزلة والخوارج عن الحق، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the proof that the name of faith does not disappear with the disappearance of justice is the saying of the Most High: 'O you who have believed, testimony between you when death approaches one of you at the time of bequest is two men of justice from among you.' If every believer were just, He would say 'two from among you'; since the beginning of the verse addresses the believers, it is established that a person may be a believer just and a believer not just. And Allah Most High said: 'O you who have believed, when you contract a debt for a specified term' until His saying: 'from those you approve of among the witnesses.' If every believer were approved, the condition would have no benefit. And likewise His saying: 'And bring to witness two witnesses of justice from among you' establishes that the believer can be just and not just. And likewise His saying: 'Then if you perceive in them sound judgment, release their property to them' establishes that among them there may be one with sound judgment and one without sound judgment. And if every believer were just, and whoever is not just is not a believer, then no testimony would be rejected due to immorality after examination, and it would not be permissible to inquire about conditions to know justice and immorality; rather, immediately one would ask about his state of faith and his ability to fulfill, and his testimony would be accepted without inquiry about him and without consideration of his conditions. And the consensus of the Community (umma) on examining conditions and not relying on what appears of wealth, which suffices in matters whose conditions are faith regarding lawful, unlawful, and inheritance, then acts of worship are proof that faith and that by which a person becomes a believer and merits its rulings is not everything that remains of types of transgression and disobedience. And there is no power except with Allah. And accordingly is the meaning of the command to this Community regarding maintaining prayers in congregations, fasting, and giving zakah despite the difference among them in violating sanctities and indulging in sins. It is established by what the Community is upon that the Mu‘tazilites and the Kharijites have deviated from the truth. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 953

Commentary
This passage presents a cumulative proof from multiple Quranic verses and from the consensus of the Muslim community to establish that the name of faith (īmān) does not disappear when a person loses justice (ʿadāla) through committing major sins. Maturidi begins with verse 5:106 regarding bequests, which requires two just witnesses from among the believers. If every believer were automatically just, the specification 'of justice' would be redundant; the phrase 'from among you' would suffice. Since the condition is stated, it implies that there are believers who are just and believers who are not just. Similarly, verse 2:282 requires witnesses 'whom you approve of' among the believers, again indicating that not every believer is approved (i.e., just). Verse 65:2 similarly calls for 'two witnesses of justice from among you,' reinforcing the same point. Verse 4:6 about orphans distinguishes between those who have sound judgment (rushd) and those who do not, yet both are believers. Then Maturidi draws a logical conclusion: if faith required justice, then no testimony would ever be rejected for immorality after verification, and there would be no need to inquire into a witness's character; one would only need to ask about his faith. But the practice of the community (ijmāʿ) is to examine witnesses' moral character and to not accept testimony based solely on apparent wealth or status, which contradicts the Mu‘tazilite position. Furthermore, the observed practice of the community—obligations of prayer, fasting, and zakah—are commanded to believers, and yet they are often negligent and sinful, showing that faith remains despite sins. This consensus and practice demonstrate that the Mu‘tazilites and Kharijites have deviated from the truth by claiming that major sins remove faith.

Key Terms

العدالة
الشهادة
الفسق
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The condition of justice in witness testimony proves that faith requires justice.
Correction: The condition actually shows that believers can be unjust; otherwise there would be no need to specify justice.

Checkpoint
What does the condition 'ذَوَا عَدْلٍ مِنْكُم' in verse 5:106 indicate according to Maturidi?

B) Believers can be either just or unjust
C) Only just people can be believers
D) Justice is the same as faith

Key Takeaway
The Quranic conditions on witnesses and the community's consensus demonstrate that a believer can be unjust and still remain a believer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Sin Does Not Negate Faith: Qur'anic and Consensus Proofs
Maturidi uses verses and consensus to argue believers can be unjust but still faithful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses Qur'anic verses and consensus to prove that committing sins does not negate a person's faith.

Core Idea
Faith remains intact even when a believer sins; the Qur'an and consensus confirm this.

Imam Maturidi argues against the Mu'tazilite and Kharijite claim that major sins remove a person from faith. He points to the Qur'an's own conditions—like requiring just witnesses—which would be meaningless if every believer were automatically just. He then invokes the consensus of the community in investigating moral character despite professed faith, and the fact that acts of worship are commanded even though believers commit sins. This passage establishes that faith and sin can coexist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the logical flow: condition → deduction → consensus → worship → conclusion.
2
Identify the two Qur'anic proofs
Find the verses about witnesses (2:282) and orphans (4:6). Understand how each implies that believers can be unjust or unsound.
The condition of justice in witnesses would be useless if all believers were just.
3
Consider the hypothetical deduction
Maturidi imagines a scenario where every believer is just. He then shows it leads to absurd consequences like no need to examine witnesses.
4
Recognize the consensus argument
The community investigates people's moral character before accepting testimony or inheritance. This proves that faith does not automatically make one just.
5
Reflect on the implication against Mu'tazila and Khawarij
Maturidi concludes that the Mu'tazila and Khawarij deviated by denying faith to sinners, since the Qur'an and practice show sin does not negate faith.
This is the key sectarian point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو كان كل مؤمن مرضيا لم يكن للشرط فائدة . وكذلك قوله : " وأَشْهدُوا ذَوِي عَدْلٍ مِنْكُمْ " ثبت أن المؤمن يكون عدلا وغير عدل، وكذلك قوله : " فَإِنَّ آنَسْتُمْ مِنْهُمْ رُشْدًا فَادْفَعُوا إِلَيْهِمْ أَمْوَالَهُمْ "، ثبت أنه قديكون منهم رشيد وغير رشيد . ولو كان كل مؤمن عدلا ، وكل من ليس بعدل ليس بمؤمن لكان لا شهادة ترد بالفسق بعد الامتحان ولا يجوز السؤال عن الأحوال ليعلم بها العدالة والفسق ، بل على المكان الذي يسأل عما عليه من الإيمان في ذلك ويمكنه الوفاء ، فيجب قبول شهادته بلا سؤال عنه ولا اعتبار بأحواله : وفى إجماع الأمة على الفحص عن الأحوال وترك النزول على ما يظهر من الأموال التي يكتفى بها فيما كان شرائطها الإيمان من الحل والحرمة والتوارث ، ثم العبادات دليل يبين أن الإيمان وما به يصير المرء مؤمنا ويستوجب أحكامه ليس هو كل ما يبقى أنواع الفسوق والعصيان ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وعلى ذلك معنى أمر هذه الأمة في تعاهد الصلوات في الجماعات والصيام وإخراج الزكوات على ما عليه هم من الاختلاف في هتك الحرمات والانهماك في المعاصى ، ثبت بالذى عليه الأمة عدول المعتزلة والخوارج عن الحق ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
If every believer were acceptable, then the condition would have no benefit. Likewise His saying: 'And bring two witnesses of justice from among you' confirms that the believer may be just or not just. Likewise His saying: 'Then if you perceive in them sound judgment, deliver to them their property' confirms that among them may be sound and not sound. And if every believer were just, and whoever is not just is not a believer, then no testimony would be rejected due to immorality after examination, nor would it be permissible to ask about conditions to know justice and immorality. Rather, one should ask about what he has of faith in that [matter] and whether he can fulfill it, so his testimony must be accepted without asking about him or considering his condition. And the consensus of the community on investigating conditions and not relying on what appears of wealth—which is sufficient for matters whose conditions are faith regarding permissibility, prohibition, and inheritance—and then acts of worship are evidence that faith and that by which a person becomes a believer and earns its rulings is not all that remains of types of immorality and disobedience. There is no power but with Allah. And upon that is the meaning of the command to this community regarding maintaining prayers in congregations, fasting, and giving zakāt despite their differences in violating sanctities and being immersed in sins. It is established by what the community is upon that the Mu'tazila and Khawārij have deviated from the truth. There is no power but with Allah.
Source pages p. 954

Commentary
This passage is a proof using Qur'anic verses and consensus to show that faith does not preclude sin. The author begins with the verse on witnesses (Surah al-Baqara 2:282) and argues that if every believer were just, the condition of justice in witnesses would be pointless. He then cites the verse on orphans (Surah an-Nisa 4:6) showing that believers can be of sound judgment or not. He draws the conclusion: if faith automatically meant justice, then no testimony would need scrutiny and no inquiry into the witness's condition would be required. But the community's consensus is to investigate moral character. Moreover, acts of worship like prayer, fasting, and zakāt are commanded despite people's sins, proving that sin does not negate faith. Therefore, the Mu'tazila and Khawārij are wrong to exclude sinners from the name of faith.

Correction: Maturidi shows through Qur'anic conditions and consensus that believers can sin and still be believers.

Maturidi's Refutation of al-Ka'bi on Repentance
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's argument from repentance verse with three responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that the command to repent proves believers commit sin, and the three arguments he uses.

Core Idea
Repentance does not prove ongoing sin; it can be for minor sins or as worship.

Al-Ka'bi argued that the Quranic command to repent proves that believers sin, since repentance is only from sin. Imam Maturidi responds by analyzing the nature of repentance and offering three compelling refutations. This lesson examines his debate and its implications for faith and sin.

Guided Path
1
Context: al-Ka'bi's challenge
Understand al-Ka'bi's argument that the verse commanding repentance (Quran 66:8) implies that believers must have sins, because repentance is only from sin.
This sets the stage for Maturidi's response.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi first presents al-Ka'bi's two possible interpretations, then gives his three responses.
Pay attention to the structure: the opponent's two ways, then three refutations.
3
Analyze Maturidi's three responses
Work through each of Maturidi's responses one by one. Understand why he rejects al-Ka'bi's interpretation and how each response undermines the argument.
The first response focuses on the meaning of repentance; the second on ingratitude; the third on the conditional nature of forgiveness.
4
Reflect on the key insight
Consider why Maturidi's refutation is significant for the relationship between faith and sin. Repentance can be commanded even for minor sins or as an act of devotion, without implying that the believer is in a state of sin.
This strengthens the position that a believer can remain faithful despite sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال لمن احتج عليه بقوله : يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا تُوبُوا إِلَى اللَّهِ تَوْبَةً ، والتوبة لا تكون إلا عن ذنب يوجهين: أحدهما أن التوبة عن الصغيرة وإن كانت مغفورة ، والثاني على التعبد كتكرار التهليل وكدعاء الملائكة بقوله : فَاغفر لِلَّذِينَ تَابُوا ۲ نقول له : الوجه الأول دل على جهله بمعنى التوبة ، إذ هى الرجوع والندم ، ومحال ذلك عما ليس عليه ، وهو مغفور له لا يجوز عليه التعذيب ، والثاني إن حق الله عليه إذا غفر له الحمد له والشكر على العفو ، وفى التوبة كفران ذلك ، يوهم بقاءه بالتوبة ، والثالث أنه قال : عَسَى رَبُّكُم أَنْ يُكَفِّرَ عَنْكُم سَيِّئَاتِكُم ۳ ، فجعله موقوفا على ما يكفره بالتوبة ، ثبت أن الذنب باق وهو لم يزل مستحقا اسم الإيمان ، والله أعلم .
He said, responding to one who argued against him with His saying: 'O you who believe, repent to Allah with a true repentance' and that repentance is only from sin, in two ways: one, that repentance is for a minor sin even if it is forgiven; two, as an act of worship like repeating the tahlīl and like the angels' supplication in His saying: 'so forgive those who repent'. We say to him: The first way indicates ignorance of the meaning of repentance, for it is return and regret, and that is impossible concerning what one is not upon, and he is forgiven and cannot be punished. The second way: indeed Allah's right over him, when He forgives him, is praise and gratitude for pardon, while in repentance there is ingratitude for that, for it implies that he remains in need of repentance. The third way: He said: 'Perhaps your Lord will remove from you your sins', making it conditional upon what He removes through repentance. This proves that the sin remains and he has not ceased to deserve the name of faith. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 955

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. Al-Ka'bi used the verse commanding repentance (Surah at-Tahrim 66:8) to argue that believers must have sin, because repentance is only from sin. Maturidi first notes that the opponent gives two possible interpretations: (1) repentance is for a minor sin even if forgiven, or (2) it is a purely devotional act like repeating the tahlīl. Then Maturidi gives three responses: First, the interpretation that it is for a minor sin shows ignorance, because repentance is regret and returning, which cannot be for a state one is not in (i.e., the sin is forgiven). Second, if it is devotional, then commanding repentance for a forgiven sin implies ingratitude for the forgiveness, suggesting the sin is not yet forgiven. Third, the verse itself makes the removal of sins conditional on repentance ('perhaps'), proving that the sin still exists. Thus, the believer remains worthy of the name of faith despite sin.

ذنب
مغفور
التعبد
تهليل
كفران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any command to repent implies the person has sinned and is currently sinful.
Correction: Repentance can be commanded for minor sins already forgiven or as an act of worship, not necessarily implying ongoing sin. Maturidi shows that the repentance verse does not prove continuous sin.

Checkpoint

A) The verse is abrogated.
B) Repentance as regret is impossible for a forgiven sin.
C) The verse only applies to polytheists.
D) Al-Ka'bi misread the Arabic grammar.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the repentance verse does not prove ongoing sin; rather, repentance can be for forgiven minor sins or as worship, and the verse itself conditions forgiveness on repentance.

Why Commanding Repentance for a Forgiven Sin is Invalid
Maturidi explains why commanding tawba for a forgiven sin is invalid and akin to inappropriate du'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is invalid to command repentance for a sin that has already been forgiven, and how this relates to ingratitude for blessings and the nature of worship.

Core Idea
Commanding repentance for a forgiven sin implies ingratitude, because it denies the forgiveness already granted.

Maturidi continues his refutation by examining the logical consequences of commanding repentance for a forgiven sin. He argues that such a command would imply the sin remains unforgiven, which is a form of ingratitude for Allah's blessings. He contrasts this with commands to recite tahlīl, which are permissible without any sin, and draws an analogy with inappropriate supplication. Let us explore this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis: repentance is only for sin
Read the first part of the passage where Maturidi states that the reality of actions is that they do not remain, and repentance is for a sin. If there is no sin (because it is forgiven), then commanding repentance is meaningless.
This sets the foundation: repentance is tied to sin, not a general act of worship.
2
Contrast with permissible worship: tahlīl
Notice that Maturidi says commanding tahlīl is permissible as an act of worship even without sin, but commanding tawba and istighfar for a forgiven sin is not because it suggests the sin is not forgiven.
Tahlīl is a pure act of devotion, while tawba inherently implies prior sin.
3
Reflect on the analogy with supplication
Consider the example of supplicating to Allah not to wrong or oppress – this is inappropriate because it implies the possibility of wrongdoing. Similarly, angels' supplication for forgiveness is only for those with unforgiven sins. This reinforces the argument.
The analogy shows that certain forms of speech can imply something false about Allah's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنه والتسهيل له في كل وقت حكم التحديد؛ لأن حقيقة الأفعال أن لا يبقى والتوبة يكون عن ذنب، ولا ذنب. وبعد، فإنه يجوز الأمر بالتهليل على التعبد ولا يجوز بالتوبة والاستغفار عن ذنب مغفور؛ لما فيه إيهام أنه ليس بمغفور، وذلك كفران النعم، وغير جائز ذلك، كما لا يجوز الدعاء بأن لا يجوز ولا يظلم، ودعاء الملائكة؛ لما قد يكون لمن ذكر ذنوبا غير مغفورة، فإلى ذلك ينصرف الدعاء، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Because ease for him at every time is the ruling of specification; for the reality of actions is that they do not remain, and repentance is for a sin, and there is no sin. Moreover, it is permissible to command tahlīl as an act of worship, and it is not permissible to command repentance and seeking forgiveness for a forgiven sin, because it implies that it is not forgiven, and that is ingratitude for blessings, and that is not permissible, just as supplication is not permissible for that He should not wrong and not oppress, and the supplication of the angels—because it may be for one who has mentioned sins that are not forgiven, so the supplication is directed to that. There is no power but with Allah.
Source pages p. 955

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the previous argument. Maturidi states that the 'ease' given to the sinner (the command to repent) is actually a specification of the ruling; it does not mean the sin remains. The reality of actions is that they do not persist. Repentance is only for a sin, but if there is no sin (because it is forgiven), commanding repentance is invalid. Then he contrasts commanding tahlīl (saying 'there is no god but Allah') as a devotional act, which is permissible even without prior sin. But commanding tawba and istighfar for a forgiven sin is not permissible because it implies that the sin is not yet forgiven, which is ingratitude for Allah's forgiveness. Similarly, it is not permissible to supplicate to Allah asking Him not to wrong or oppress, because that would imply He could do so. The angels' supplication for forgiveness is directed only to those who have unforgiven sins, not to those already forgiven. Thus, the verse commanding repentance cannot be used to say believers commit sins.

Key Terms
توبة
استغفار
تهليل
كفران النعم
دعاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commanding repentance for a forgiven sin is acceptable because it is a form of worship.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such a command implies that the sin is not yet forgiven, which is ingratitude for the blessing of forgiveness. Worship like tahlīl does not imply sin and is permissible.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider commanding repentance for a forgiven sin to be 'ingratitude for blessings' (kufrān al-ni'am)?
A) Because it suggests that Allah's forgiveness is insufficient.
B) Because it implies the sin is not yet forgiven.
C) Because it rejects the obligation to thank Allah.
D) Because it is a form of polytheism.

Key Takeaway
Commanding repentance for a sin that Allah has already forgiven is invalid because it implies ingratitude for the forgiveness, just as asking Allah not to wrong implies the possibility of wrongdoing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about a past sin that you have sincerely repented from, do you still feel the need to constantly seek forgiveness for it? How does holding onto that guilt or re-repenting reflect ingratitude for Allah's complete forgiveness?
?
In your du'a, do you ever ask Allah not to wrong you or to be fair to you? What does that reveal about your belief in Allah's justice and mercy, and how can you rephrase your supplications to avoid implying ingratitude or lack of trust?
?
The lesson says that commanding tawba for a forgiven sin is like kufr al-ni'm. Are you truly living as one who has been fully pardoned, or do you habitually return to old 'used-to-be-sins' in your mind as if they still define you? What would it mean for you today to accept that His forgiveness is final?
