Misconception: Some think that if major sinners are included in the legal rights of faith, then they must be considered true believers (mu'minun) in the theological sense.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that inclusion in legal rights is based on communal consensus (ijma'), not on the name of faith itself. The legal rulings apply by agreement of the community, not because the sinner deserves the title of believer.

The Objection of the Madman and Child and the Validity of Worship
Maturidi refutes objections about the rulings of madmen and children and the validity of worship for sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes objections that attempt to compare the major sinner to a madman or child, and how he establishes that acts of worship are valid only due to faith.

Core Idea
Faith alone makes worship valid, not external factors.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses two related objections. The first misapplies the rulings of madmen and children to argue that the major sinner's worship is not due to his own faith. The second questions the purpose of prayer and fasting for a sinner. Maturidi clarifies that both objections stem from a misunderstanding: acts of worship are valid only because of the presence of faith, not because of any external benefit.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first objection about the madman and child
Read the opening lines where the objector argues that the rulings for the madman and child are due to external factors, and concludes that the sinner deserves his worship by his own faith. Notice the objector's reasoning.
The objector tries to draw an analogy between the madman/child and the major sinner.
2
Analyze the second objection about prayer and fasting
Read the next part where another objection is raised: the sinner's prayer and fasting are only to prevent an increase in sin. Consider how this objection challenges the validity of the sinner's worship.
3
Understand the shaykh's clarification
Read the shaykh's response that the objector misunderstood. The acts are permitted by faith alone; without faith, they would not be valid. Reflect on how this answers both objections.
The shaykh's commentary is part of the source, so it is authoritative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعارض فى التحليل بالمجنون والصغير، قيل: لها حكم الإيمان بغيرهما؛ إذ لولا ذلك الغير لم يجب لها ذلك، كما لو لم يجب لأولاد الكفرة وما نحن فيه لا غير فى ذلك يتبعه، فثبت أنه استوجبه بإيمان نفسه. ثم عورض بما يصلى فقال: لئلا يزداد فسقه ويزول عنه عذاب تركها. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: يقال له: لم يفهم السؤال، وإنما معنى ذلك أنهما يجوزان بالإيمان، فلولا أنه مؤمن لم يكن ليمضيا له ويزيلا عذاب تركها عنه، بل لا شيء عليه في تركها، ولا يجوز أن يفعله لو لم يكن مؤمنا، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد أفردنا في بعض كتبنا في هذه الآيات كتابا أغنانا عن الإطناب في هذا الباب
And he objected regarding analysis with the madman and the child. It was said: The ruling of iman is for them due to other than them; for if not for that other, it would not be obligatory for them, just as it is not obligatory for the children of the disbelievers. And our subject has no other in that to follow. So it is established that he deserved it by his own iman. Then he was objected to regarding what he prayed, so he said: Lest his sin increase and the punishment of abandoning it be removed from him. The shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is said to him: He did not understand the question. Rather the meaning is that both (prayer and fasting) are permitted by faith. For if he were not a believer, they would not be valid for him and would not remove the punishment of abandoning them from him. Rather, there would be nothing upon him for abandoning them, and it is not permissible for him to do them if he were not a believer. And there is no power except with Allah. And we have devoted a book in some of our works on these verses, which suffices us from elaboration in this chapter.
Source pages p. 958

Commentary
This passage deals with another objection: if a madman or a child is not held accountable, then why should a major sinner be held accountable? Maturidi responds that the rulings for the madman and child are due to their lack of capacity, not because of iman. The major sinner, however, has iman, so his acts of worship are valid and counted. Then there is an objection that the sinner's prayer and fasting are to prevent an increase in sin, but the reply clarifies that these acts are only valid because of his iman. The shaykh (a later commentator) notes that the original questioner misunderstood; the acts are permitted because of iman alone.

Key Terms
المجنون
الصغير
حكم الإيمان
صلاة
صوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The major sinner's acts of worship are not valid because he is not fully accountable, similar to a madman or child.
Correction: The passage clarifies that worship is valid only due to the presence of faith, and that the rulings for madmen and children are due to lack of capacity, not lack of faith.

Checkpoint

A) His fear of punishment
B) His faith
C) His lack of sin
D) His external circumstances

Key Takeaway
Acts of worship are valid only through faith, regardless of the performer's sinfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Maturidi's Refutation on Tahlil and the Insane/Minor
Maturidi answers an objection about tahlil involving the insane and minor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's refutation of the objection that a fasiq's religious actions are valid only by virtue of others' faith, and why his own faith is essential.

Core Idea
A person's own faith, not another's, makes their religious actions valid and punishable.

Imam Maturidi here addresses a subtle objection: if an action like prayer is valid for a fasiq (grave sinner), could its validity depend on the faith of others, as with the insane and minors? Maturidi clarifies that this misunderstanding arises from not grasping the fundamental role of personal faith. Read on to see his decisive answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
First, read the objection that compares the fasiq to the insane and minor, and the initial response that the fasiq deserves the ruling by his own faith.
Notice how the objector uses analogy to challenge the role of personal faith.
2
Study the counter-objection about prayer
The objector then brings an example of prayer, suggesting it is valid for the fasiq. The initial answer says it is to prevent increase in fisq.
3
Focus on Maturidi's final clarification
Maturidi states that the questioner misunderstood. He explains that both actions are permissible only because of the person's own faith; without it, they would not be valid and there would be no punishment for abandoning them.
This is the core refutation: personal faith is necessary.
4
Connect to the broader principle
Reflect on how this defends the central role of personal belief in Islamic theology against those who would diminish it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفاسق ويصوم الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي وعارض فى التحليل بالمجنون والصغير ، قيل : لها حكم الإيمان بغيرهما ؛ إذ لولا ذلك الغير لم يجب لها ذلك ، كما لو لم يجب لأولاد الكفرة وما نحن فيه لا غير فى ذلك يتبعه ، فثبت أنه استوجبه بإيمان نفسه . ثم عورض بما يصلى فقال : لئلا يزداد فسقه ويزول عنه عذاب تركها . قال الشيخ رحمه الله : يقال له : لم يفهم السؤال ، وإنما معنى ذلك أنهما يجوزان بالإيمان ، فلولا أنه مؤمن لم يكن ليمضيا له ويزيلا عذاب تركها عنه ، بل لا شيء عليه في تركها ، ولا يجوز أن يفعله لو لم يكن مؤمنا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وقد أفردنا في بعض كتبنا في هذه الآيات كتابا أغنانا عن الإطناب في هذا الباب
The fasiq (sinner) and fasting. Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. And he objected in the matter of tahlil (declaring permissible) by [citing] the insane and the minor. It was said: They have the ruling of faith by virtue of others; because were it not for that other, it would not be incumbent upon them, just as it is not incumbent upon the children of the disbelievers, and what we are discussing follows nothing other than that in that regard. So it is established that he [the fasiq] deserved it [the ruling] by his own faith. Then he was objected to with regard to what he prayed, so he said: Lest his fisq (immorality) increase and the punishment of abandoning it be removed from him. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is said to him: He did not understand the question. Rather the meaning of that is that both [actions] are permissible by virtue of faith. Were he not a believer, they would not be carried out for him and the punishment of abandoning them would not be removed from him; rather, there is nothing upon him for abandoning them, and it is not permissible for him to do them if he were not a believer. And there is no power except through Allah. And we have devoted a book in some of our writings concerning these verses, which sufficed us from elaboration in this chapter.
Source pages p. 959

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the role of faith in the validity of certain actions. The objector argues that an action ruled permissible (tahlil) for the fasiq (grave sinner) is based on the faith of others, similar to how the insane and minors are ruled by the faith of others. Maturidi responds by clarifying that the questioner misunderstood: the actions are only permissible because of the person's own faith. Without faith, they would not be valid, and there would be no punishment for abandoning them. The passage then refers to a separate book for fuller treatment.

Key Terms
الفاسق (al-fāsiq)
التحليل (al-taḥlīl)
المجنون (al-majnūn)
الصغير (al-ṣaghīr)
الإيمان (al-īmān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A fasiq's religious actions (like prayer) are valid because of the faith of others or because of their utilitarian benefit (e.g., preventing increase in sin).
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that all such actions are valid only due to the person's own faith. Without personal faith, the actions are not accepted and there is no punishment for abandoning them.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's response, why are actions like prayer valid for a fasiq?
A) Because of the faith of others
B) Because of his own faith
C) Because he is compelled by law
D) Because he fears punishment

Key Takeaway
Maturidi emphasizes that without one's own faith, religious actions have no validity and carry no consequence, defending the centrality of personal belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely on being part of a Muslim family or community to feel your worship is accepted, rather than checking the state of your own iman? If your personal faith were to weaken, would your prayers still be valid? What will you do today to strengthen your own faith, not just your outward practice?
?
The passage says that without personal faith, there is no punishment for abandoning acts—but also no reward. Are you content with performing rituals out of habit or social pressure, knowing they may count for nothing if your heart is not with them? What empty act will you transform into a sincere one this week?
?
When you sin, do you assume that as a Muslim you are still 'covered' by the faith of your parents or community? This lesson insists that only your own faith makes your actions count. Will you continue in complacency, or take responsibility for cultivating your iman as a matter of life and death?

The Good Deeds of the Believer Outweigh His Sins
Maturidi argues that the sinner's good deeds outweigh his sins, preventing eternal punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that a believer's good deeds outweigh his major sins (excluding shirk), preventing eternal punishment, and why this does not apply to disbelievers.

Core Idea
The believer's good deeds tip the scales against eternal punishment.

Maturidi presents the first of two ways to distinguish eternal from non-eternal punishment based on wisdom. He argues that a believer who commits major sins (excluding shirk) still performs acts of obedience that constitute good deeds, and these outweigh his sins. Therefore, eternal punishment would be unjust. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: first a principle, then an application to the believer, then a contrast with the disbeliever.
The passage uses a conditional argument: if good deeds outweigh sins, then eternal punishment is unjust.
2
Identify the key distinction
Maturidi distinguishes between eternal and non-eternal punishment from the perspective of wisdom. Find where he states that punishment is based on what wisdom necessitates, not on arbitrary choice.
3
Understand the weighing argument
The believer's good deeds include fear of punishment, hope for mercy, trust in generosity, and withdrawal from wrath. Maturidi argues that these outweigh the evil of the sins, so depriving the believer of the benefit of those good deeds would be unjust.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Contrast with the disbeliever
Maturidi explicitly states that the disbeliever has no good deeds that can weigh against his sins. Therefore, eternal punishment for the disbeliever does not contradict generosity and nobility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تذكر الفصل بين ما يخلد له العذاب ولا يخلد من طريق الحكمة وذلك يخرج على وجهين : أحدهما من طريق الاعتبار بتفاوت الذنوب في أنفسها وقد وعد الله أن لا يجزى إلا مثله ، وكذلك حق الحكمة ؛ إذ التعذيب يكون بما يوجبه الحكمة لا بما يختار ؛ إذ ليس هو نوع ما يختار وبخاصة ممن لا يضره الخلاف ، ثم هو الموصوف بالعفو والرحمة ، ولهذا ما أوجب المغفرة والعفو عن كثير من الذنوب . ثم يخرج ذا على وجوه : أحدها أنه ما أحد من يعصي الله بنوع من الكبائر دون الشرك إلا وهو لوقت العصيان مكتسب الطاعة من خوف عقاب ،] والنزع عن مقته ، ورجاء رحمته ، والثقة بكرمه ، / وذلك عن خيرات لو قوبل بها ما ارتكب من الخلاف بغلبة شهوة وقهر غضب أو نحو ذلك ليرجح ما كان له منه من خير على ما كان من شر ، فلا يجوز ، فلا يجوز أن يحرم نفع الخير ويوجب عقوبة الشر ، ومن ذلك فعله موصوف بالجود والكرم ولا كذلك معناهما ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وليس مع من يكفر بالله ويشرك به معنى يستحق اسم الحسنة والخير ؛ لأنه يكذبه وينكر أمره ونهيه ، فلا يحتمل أن يكون له الرجا ، وفى دوام عذابه مضادة معنى الكرم والجود ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then mention the distinction between that for which punishment is eternal and that which is not eternal from the way of wisdom. That is derived in two ways: One of them is by way of considering the disparity of sins in themselves, and Allah has promised that He will not requite except with its like, and such is the right of wisdom. For punishment comes from what wisdom necessitates, not from what one chooses, since it is not a type of choice, especially from one whom opposition does not harm. Then He is described with pardon and mercy, and because of that He has made forgiveness and pardon obligatory for many sins. Then this is derived in aspects: One of them is that there is no one who disobeys Allah by a kind of major sin except shirk, except that at the time of disobedience he is acquiring obedience from fear of punishment, and withdrawal from His wrath, and hope for His mercy, and trust in His generosity, and that is from good deeds which, if balanced against what he committed of opposition due to overwhelming desire or overpowering anger or the like, would outweigh the evil he has from it. So it is not permissible, it is not permissible to deprive him of the benefit of good and impose the punishment of evil. And because of that His action is described with generosity and nobility, but not so their meaning. And there is no power except through Allah. And there is not with the one who disbelieves in Allah and associates partners with Him any meaning that deserves the name of good deed or goodness, because he denies Him and rejects His command and prohibition. So it is not possible that he has hope, and in the permanence of his punishment there is contradiction to the meaning of generosity and nobility. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 959

Commentary
This passage defines the first way of distinguishing eternal punishment from non-eternal punishment based on wisdom. The argument proceeds: Allah has promised proportionate requital, and wisdom dictates that punishment is based on necessity, not arbitrary choice. The sinner who commits major sins (excluding shirk) nonetheless performs acts of obedience (fear, hope, trust) which constitute good deeds. When balanced against his sins, these good deeds outweigh the evil. Therefore, it is not just to deprive him of the benefit of those good deeds and impose eternal punishment. In contrast, the disbeliever lacks any good deeds, so eternal punishment does not contradict generosity and nobility. The passage contrasts the two cases explicitly.

Key Terms
الخلود (al-khulūd)
العذاب (al-ʿadhāb)
الحكمة (al-ḥikmah)
الكبائر (al-kabāʾir)
الشرك (al-shirk)
الحسنة (al-ḥasanah)
الجود (al-jūd)
الكرم (al-karam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All major sins lead to eternal punishment regardless of the person's faith.
Correction: Maturidi shows that a believer's good deeds (fear, hope, trust) outweigh his major sins (excluding shirk), making eternal punishment unjust. Only disbelief warrants eternal punishment.

Checkpoint
What is the key reason given that a believer who commits major sins (excluding shirk) does not deserve eternal punishment?
A) Allah is merciful regardless of deeds
B) Their good deeds outweigh their sins
C) They will repent after death
D) The punishment is only for disbelievers

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that a believer's good deeds, motivated by fear and hope, outweigh his major sins, so eternal punishment is unjust; only disbelief warrants eternal punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you feel the fear of Allah's punishment and hope for His mercy? Or do you sin as if you are alone, forgetting that He sees you? This lesson says that even in your disobedience, these feelings are good deeds that outweigh your sin. But if you sin without any fear or hope, what does that say about your faith? Will you stop and check your heart the next time you are tempted?
?
Maturidi argues that a believer's good deeds outweigh his sins, but only if he has those good deeds from fear, hope, and trust. Have you relied on the mercy of Allah to excuse your sins without actually having that inner state? This lesson demands that you examine your motives. Are you truly hoping for His mercy or just taking it for granted? Commit today to recite His attributes and feel them before you act.
?
The disbeliever has no good deeds because he denies Allah. But what about you? Do you sometimes act as if Allah does not see you, even for a moment? That is a form of denial in action. This lesson warns that without the good deeds that come from acknowledging Allah, your deeds are empty. Spend a minute now asking Allah to fill your heart with fear and hope so that your actions are weighed in your favor.

Second Way: Proportionate Requital for Disbelief
Maturidi argues that eternal punishment is proportionate to the infinite gravity of disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that eternal punishment for disbelief is proportionate because shirk is the greatest sin and the disbeliever has no good deeds to offset it.

Core Idea
Shirk's infinite gravity demands eternal punishment; no good deeds exist to balance it.

Maturidi now presents a second argument for why disbelief leads to eternal punishment. He grounds it in the principle of proportionate requital: Allah's justice requires that punishment fit the crime. Since shirk is the greatest sin and the disbeliever has no good deeds at all, the only just requital is eternity in the Fire. This lesson unpacks that careful reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi builds his case: from Allah's promise, to the gravity of shirk, to the absence of good deeds, then to the conclusion of eternity.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: 'ف' (so) and 'إذ' (for).
2
Identify the key premises
List out the premises Maturidi uses: Allah's promise of proportionate requital, shirk being the greatest sin in reason, the disbeliever having no good deeds, and the thought experiment about accepting multiple punishments for one day of salvation.
Each premise supports the conclusion that eternal punishment is just.
3
Understand the supporting argument
Maturidi strengthens his case by noting that a disbeliever would accept far greater punishment for just one day of relief. This shows that the intended punishment (eternity) is actually what is due.
This step uses a hypothetical to illustrate proportionality.
4
Contrast with lesser sins
Maturidi then distinguishes disbelievers from those who commit lesser sins but have good deeds. He concludes that for disbelievers, with no good deeds, only eternity suffices.
This contrast clarifies that the argument is specific to shirk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن الله جل ثناؤه وعدا أن لا يجزى إلا مثلها ، ومثل الشرك الذي في العقل أكبر من كل ذنب ، مع ما لا حسنة يكون معه ومع غيره ، إنما هو الخلود في النار ؛ إذ معلوم أن الكافر يرضى بأضعاف ما يعذب مع النجاة يوما من الدهر ، فيبين ذلك أن تمام جزائه الخلود ، فإذا كان لغيره مثله فيجزى غيره أكثر من مثل الفعل ، وذلك جوار فى حكمته ، والله يجل عنه ، فهذا ما كان مرتكب ما دونه حسنات، وليس معه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And the second: That Allah, may His glory be exalted, promised that He will not requite except with its like. And the like of shirk (associating partners), which in reason is greater than every sin, together with the fact that there is no good deed with him nor with others, is only eternity in the Fire. For it is known that the disbeliever would accept multiple times what he is punished with along with salvation for one day of time. This shows that his complete requital is eternity. If another had his like, then he [the other] would be requited with more than the like of the act, and that is injustice in His wisdom, and Allah is exalted above that. So this is what one who commits what is less than it has good deeds, and he has none. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 960

Commentary
This passage presents the second way of distinguishing eternal punishment: the principle of proportionate requital. Maturidi argues that shirk is the greatest sin in reason, and the disbeliever has no good deeds. Therefore, the only just requital for shirk is eternal punishment in the Fire. If someone else committed a sin of equal magnitude (which is impossible except for another disbeliever), they would receive more than the like, which would be unjust. So the complete requital for disbelief is eternity, while those who commit lesser sins have good deeds to mitigate. The argument relies on the proportionality and the absence of good deeds for the disbeliever.

Key Terms
الشرك (al-shirk)
الخلود (al-khulūd)
الجزاء (al-jazāʾ)
المثل (al-mithl)
الحسنة (al-ḥasanah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any major sin could warrant eternal punishment.
Correction: Maturidi specifies that only shirk has no good deeds and is the greatest sin in reason, so eternal punishment is unique to disbelief.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that the complete requital for shirk is eternity in the Fire?
A) Because shirk is the only sin
B) Because shirk has no like and the disbeliever has no good deeds
C) Because Allah is angry with disbelievers
D) Because the disbeliever will not repent

Key Takeaway
Eternal punishment for disbelief is just because shirk is the greatest sin and the disbeliever has no good deeds to mitigate it, making eternity the only proportionate requital.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every sin you commit without iman is recorded with no good deed to balance it, how would your daily actions change? What one thing will you start doing today to ensure your deeds are built on a firm foundation of tawheed?
?
The disbeliever in this thought experiment would accept multiple punishments for just one day of salvation. Does your own urgency to call others to the truth reflect this reality? Whom will you speak to about Islam this week, even if it costs you comfort?
?
Allah's justice is perfectly proportionate, as Maturidi argues. Is there an area of your life where you secretly doubt that Allah's judgment is fair? How can you actively submit your heart to His wisdom, even when you don't understand your trials?

The Expiation Argument: Hudud and Eternal Punishment
Maturidi uses hudud as expiations to argue for eternal punishment of disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the expiatory nature of legal punishments (hudud) to argue for the eternal punishment of disbelief.

Core Idea
If hudud are expiations, disbelief must have no expiation, so its punishment is eternal.

Maturidi presents a logical argument from the nature of legal punishments (hudud) in Islam. He observes that these punishments serve as expiations for sins. This leads to a key insight about disbelief: since there is no expiation for disbelief in this world, its punishment must be eternal, unlike other sins which have limited punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The argument uses conditional reasoning: if not expiation, then absurd.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that Maturidi states that the hudud are made expiations for sins. This is the foundational point.
This premise is taken from Islamic law and tradition.
3
Follow the conditional reasoning
Consider the hypothetical: if hudud did not expiate, they would increase punishment for disbelief, which is impossible because lesser sins cannot have greater punishment.
The impossibility shows that hudud must be expiations.
4
Understand the conclusion about disbelief
Since there is no expiation for disbelief in this world, its punishment cannot be limited. Therefore, it is eternal.
This contrasts with other sins which have a limited punishment (hadd).
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument reinforces the severity of disbelief compared to other sins.
Maturidi uses this to support the doctrine of eternal punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الحدود في الدنيا جعلن كفارات لما يرتكب من الذنوب ، فلو لم يكن فيها تكفير كانت تكون زيادات على عقوبات الكفر ، ومحال أن يزداد عقوبة ما دون الكفر ، فثبت أنها كفارات ، ولا كفارة للكفر في الدنيا ، ثبت أنه لا يحتمل في العقوبة فجعلت عقوبته أبدية وعقوبة غيره بحد ، فكذلك العقوبة الموعودة فيه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also that the legal punishments (ḥudūd) in this world are made expiations (kaffārāt) for sins that are committed. For if they did not involve expiation, they would be additions to the punishments of disbelief, and it is impossible that the punishment for what is less than disbelief increases. So it is established that they are expiations, and there is no expiation for disbelief in this world. It is established that it [disbelief] does not bear [limited] punishment, so its punishment is made eternal, and the punishment of other than it is [by] a limit. And likewise is the promised punishment in it. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 960

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the legal punishments (ḥudūd) in Islamic law. Maturidi argues that these punishments serve as expiations for sins. If they did not expiate, they would be additional punishments for the disbeliever, which is absurd because punishments for lesser sins cannot exceed those for disbelief. Therefore, the ḥudūd are expiations. However, there is no expiation for disbelief in this world, so the punishment for disbelief must be eternal. This contrasts with other sins which have a limited punishment (the ḥadd) in this world, implying a limited punishment in the Hereafter as well. Thus, the promised punishment for disbelief is eternal.

Key Terms
الحدود (al-ḥudūd)
الكفارات (al-kaffārāt)
الكفر (al-kufr)
العقوبة (al-ʿuqūbah)
الأبدية (al-abadiyyah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that ḥudūd are purely punitive, with no expiatory function.
Correction: Maturidi argues that ḥudūd are expiations; otherwise they would absurdly increase punishment for disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, why is disbelief punished eternally while other sins have limited punishment?
A) Because disbelief is the only sin without expiation in this world
B) Because disbelievers never repent
C) Because ḥudūd are not applied to disbelievers
D) Because Allah is more severe with disbelievers

Key Takeaway
The expiatory function of hudud implies that disbelief, having no expiation, must be punished eternally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly appreciate that every sin you commit after faith could be expiated through worldly trials or punishments, while disbelief has no such mercy? How does this reality shape your fear of losing faith and your gratitude for being guided?
?
When you hear about eternal punishment for disbelief, does it ever make you complacent about your own minor sins? Or does it drive you to take your faith more seriously, knowing that the greatest catastrophe is to die upon disbelief?
?
If the hudud are expiatory, what does that say about God's mercy and justice? Have you ever felt that God's punishments in this life are harsh, or do you now see them as a blessing that spares you from greater punishment? How will this change the way you respond to trials?

Double Punishment for Misleaders Only
Maturidi refutes the idea that all major sins double punishment, using Quranic specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that only leading others astray, not committing major sins in general, carries a doubled punishment.

Core Idea
Doubling of punishment is specific to misguidance, not to every major sin.

Maturidi presents a crucial distinction between the punishment for disbelief itself and the punishment for additional sins. Using the Quranic promise of double punishment for those who disbelieve and mislead, he argues that major sins do not automatically double one’s punishment. This distinction protects the Islamic understanding of justice and the specificity of divine threats.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the Quranic quotes and the logical flow of Maturidi's reasoning.
Focus on the conditional 'if...then' structure in the middle.
2
Identify the key claim and evidence
Maturidi states that Allah promises double punishment to those who disbelieve and mislead others. Then he argues: if major sins doubled punishment, every disbeliever would get double; but Allah specifically singles out misleaders.
The Quranic verses (29:13 and 7:38) are his evidence for specificity.
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that major sins do not automatically cause doubling. He also contrasts the punishment of disbelievers (all sins) with that of believers (not all sins).
This reinforces that disbelief is the foundation of eternal punishment, while lesser sins are treated differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الله جل ثناؤه أخبر أن الموعودة عقوبة الذين كفروا وأضلوا غيرهم ضعف عقوبة من كفر ولم يضل غيره ، ثم لو كان للكافر عقوبة غير الإضلال مثل عقوبة الإضلال لكان كل كافر عقوبته مضاعفة ؛ لأنه لا كافر إلا ومعه سوى الكفر - كبائر ، وقد خص الله بالمضاعفة المضلين بقوله : وَلَيَحْمِلُنَّ أَثْقَالَهُمْ وَأَثْقَالًا مَعَ أَنْقَالِهِم ، وقول الأتباع : رَبَّنَا هَوْلَاء أَضَلُّونَا ، وجعل لكل ضعفا ، فبطل أن يكون ذلك عقوبة الكبيرة ، بل هي لو كانت في الكفر كان أحق للضعف منه فى الإسلام للمثل ، ألا ترى أنه يعاقب الكافر بجميع الآثام من صغائر وكبائر ، ولا كذلك أمر من اعتقد دين الإسلام ، ولا قوة الا بالله
And also that Allah, may His glory be exalted, informed that the promised punishment of those who disbelieve and mislead others is double the punishment of one who disbelieves but does not mislead others. Then, if the disbeliever had a punishment other than misguidance like the punishment for misguidance, then every disbeliever would have a doubled punishment, because there is no disbeliever except that with him besides disbelief there are major sins. And Allah specifically singled out the misleaders for doubling by His saying: 'They will certainly bear their own burdens and [other] burdens along with their burdens' (29:13), and the saying of the followers: 'Our Lord, these are they who misled us' (7:38), and He made for each a double. So it is invalid that that is a punishment for the major sin. Rather, if it [the major sin] were in disbelief, it would be more deserving of doubling than in Islam for the like. Do you not see that He punishes the disbeliever for all sins, small and great, but not so for the one who professes the religion of Islam. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 960

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the Quran. Allah states that those who disbelieve and mislead others receive double punishment. If major sins (other than disbelief) also carried a doubled punishment, then every disbeliever would have double punishment because they all commit major sins. But Allah specifically attributes doubling to misleaders, not to all disbelievers. Therefore, major sins do not automatically double the punishment. The disbeliever is punished for all his sins (major and minor), but the believer who commits major sins is not punished for every sin in the same way. This reinforces that the punishment for disbelief is eternal and distinct, while lesser sins have different treatment.

Key Terms
المضاعفة (al-muḍāʿafah)
الإضلال (al-iḍlāl)
الكبائر (al-kabāʾir)
الصغائر (al-ṣaghāʾir)
الأثقال (al-athqāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all major sins are punished with double the punishment in the afterlife.
Correction: Maturidi shows from the Quran that double punishment is specifically for those who disbelieve and mislead others, not for mere major sins.

Checkpoint
What does the Quranic verse about double punishment for misleaders prove according to Maturidi?
A) That major sins always double punishment
B) That only misguidance doubles punishment, not major sins in general
C) That all disbelievers get double punishment
D) That believers are not punished for sins

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that only leading others astray, not committing major sins in general, results in doubled punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Second Argument: God's Promise of Just Recompense
Maturidi presents a proof from God's promise of exact recompense for kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's proof that kufr deserves eternal punishment based on God's promise of exact recompense and the unique severity of shirk.

Core Idea
Shirk's unique enormity demands eternal punishment, as no good deeds can counterbalance it.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi presents his second argument for the eternal punishment of disbelief. He draws on God's promise to requite every deed justly and argues that shirk is so severe that no good deed can accompany it, so its only just recompense is eternity in the Fire. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own logic come through.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that the argument starts with God's promise to requite every deed with its like. This is the foundational premise.
3
Understand why shirk is unique
The author claims that shirk is the greatest sin and that no good deed can accompany it. Consider why this is so in Islamic theology.
Shirk nullifies all good deeds, leaving no counterbalance.
4
Follow the supporting reasoning
The author uses a hypothetical: a disbeliever would accept multiplied punishment in exchange for one day of salvation. This illustrates that the only just recompense for kufr is eternity.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The final sentence contrasts the kafir with committers of lesser sins, who may have good deeds. The kafir has none, so his punishment is eternal.
This argument distinguishes kufr from other sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن الله جل ثناؤه وعدا أن لا يجزى إلا مثلها ، ومثل الشرك الذي في العقل أكبر من كل ذنب ، مع ما لا حسنة يكون معه ومع غيره ، إنما هو الخلود في النار ؛ إذ معلوم أن الكافر يرضى بأضعاف ما يعذب مع النجاة يوما من الدهر ، فيبين ذلك أن تمام جزائه الخلود ، فإذا كان لغيره مثله فيجزى غيره أكثر من مثل الفعل ، وذلك جوار فى حكمته ، والله يجل عنه ، فهذا ما كان مرتكب ما دونه حسنات، وليس معه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And the second: that God, magnificent is His praise, promised that He will not requite except with its like, and the likeness of shirk in the intellect is greater than every sin, along with which no good deed can be with it or with other than it; it is but eternal abiding in the Fire. For it is known that the disbeliever would accept multiple times the punishment he suffers along with salvation for a day of time, so that shows that the completion of his requital is eternity. If another had the like of it, then another would be requited with more than the like of the act, and that is injustice in His wisdom, and God is exalted above that. So this is the state of the committer of what is less than it—good deeds, and not with it—and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 960

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (following an unstated first) for the eternal punishment of kufr. It is a proof from God's promise of exact recompense. The author states that God promised to requite each deed with its like. The likeness of shirk (associating partners with God) in the intellect is greater than any sin, and no good deed can accompany it or be with anything other than it—meaning shirk nullifies all good deeds. Therefore its requital is eternal abiding in the Fire. The proof proceeds by noting that a disbeliever would willingly accept multiplied punishment in exchange for even one day of salvation, which shows that the full requital must be eternal; if another sin had the same weight, the punishment would exceed the act's due, implying injustice in God's wisdom—which is impossible. Thus the committer of lesser sins (below kufr) may have good deeds, but the kafir has none, so his punishment is eternal.

Key Terms
الخلود
الجزاء
الشرك
الكافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's promise of exact recompense implies that all sins, including shirk, are punished proportionally and not necessarily eternally.
Correction: Maturidi argues that shirk is uniquely severe because no good deeds can accompany it, so its just recompense is eternal, unlike lesser sins which may be balanced by good deeds.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, why is the punishment for kufr eternal?
A) Because God promised to requite every deed exactly, and shirk is the greatest sin with no accompanying good deeds.
B) Because the disbeliever refuses to repent.
C) Because God is angry with the disbeliever.
D) Because the disbeliever's evil deeds outweigh his good deeds.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that kufr deserves eternal punishment because shirk is the gravest sin, with no possible good deeds to counterbalance it, and God's justice requires exact recompense.

?

What subtle forms of shirk in your own life have you tolerated as 'minor'? Will you commit this week to identifying and repenting from them with the same urgency as if they were the greatest sin?

Maturidi argues that God's justice requires exactly matching punishment. If you truly believe God will requite every deed with its like, how does that change the way you evaluate your own actions today? Worldly Hadd Punishments as Expiation
Maturidi argues that hudud are expiations for sins, not for kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's argument that worldly hudud serve as expiations for sins, and why this implies that kufr has no worldly expiation and its punishment is eternal.

Core Idea
Worldly hudud expiate sins, but kufr has no expiation, so its punishment is eternal.

Imam Maturidi presents a subtle argument from analogy. He points out that the fixed penalties (hudud) established in Islamic law are not merely punitive; they are expiations for the sins they address. If they were not expiatory, they would add punishment on top of the punishment for disbelief – a logical impossibility. This leads to a critical conclusion about the nature of kufr: because it has no worldly expiation, its punishment must be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own line of reasoning come through.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if not... then... impossible'.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break the argument into its premises: (1) Hudud are expiations. (2) If not, they'd be additions to kufr's punishment. (3) That is impossible. (4) Therefore they are expiations. (5) Kufr has no expiation, so its punishment is eternal.
The argument relies on an analogy with worldly penalties.
3
Reflect on the uniqueness of kufr
Consider why kufr is singled out: no worldly expiation means no limited punishment; only eternal punishment fits.
This ties into Maturidi's broader theology of divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع وأيضا أن الحدود في الدنيا جعلن كفارات لما يرتكب من الذنوب ، فلو لم يكن فيها تكفير كانت تكون زيادات على عقوبات الكفر ، ومحال أن يزداد عقوبة ما دون الكفر ، فثبت أنها كفارات ، ولا كفارة للكفر في الدنيا ، ثبت أنه لا يحتمل في العقوبة فجعلت عقوبته أبدية وعقوبة غيره بحد ، فكذلك العقوبة الموعودة فيه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also: that the fixed legal penalties (al-hudud) in the world were made as expiations for the sins committed. If there were no expiation in them, they would be additions to the punishments of kufr, and it is impossible that the punishment of what is below kufr be increased. So it is established that they are expiations. And there is no expiation for kufr in the world, so it is established that it does not admit of [limited] punishment; thus its punishment was made eternal, and the punishment of other than it is by a [fixed] measure. So likewise is the promised punishment for it, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 960

Commentary
This passage provides an additional proof within the second argument. It is an argument from analogy with worldly legal penalties (hudud). The author states that hudud in this world were instituted as expiations (kaffarat) for sins. If they were not expiatory, they would be additional punishments on top of the punishment for kufr—an absurdity because sins below kufr cannot have a greater punishment than kufr itself. Therefore hudud are expiations. Since kufr has no expiation in this world, it does not admit of a limited punishment; thus its punishment is eternal, while the punishment for other sins is fixed by a measure. So the promised punishment for kufr is eternal.

Key Terms
الحدود
الكفارات
الكفر
العقوبة الأبدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hudud are merely punitive and have no expiatory function.
Correction: Maturidi argues that hudud are indeed expiations; otherwise they would unjustly add to the punishment for kufr.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the fact that there is no worldly expiation for kufr?
A) That kufr is unpardonable in the afterlife.
B) That kufr's punishment must be eternal.
C) That kufr is a minor sin.
D) That the hudud are not effective.

Key Takeaway
The eternal punishment of kufr is deduced from the absence of any worldly expiation for disbelief.

Double Punishment: Kufr vs. Misguidance
Maturidi uses Quranic evidence to prove distinct punishments for kufr and misguidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic verses to prove that double punishment is specific to those who combine disbelief with misguiding others, and not a general punishment for all disbelievers.

Core Idea
Double punishment is for misguidance, not for kufr alone.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi presents a nuanced argument from the Quran to distinguish between the punishment for disbelief and the punishment for misleading others. He shows that God's promise of double punishment is reserved for those who not only disbelieve but also lead others astray. This lesson will walk you through his reasoning and the scriptural evidence he provides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow and the two Quranic verses quoted.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between two groups: those who disbelieve and mislead, and those who disbelieve without misleading. Focus on what punishment each group receives according to the Quran.
3
Examine the conditional argument
Maturidi presents a conditional: if kufr itself carried the same punishment as misguidance, then every disbeliever would receive double punishment (since all disbelievers commit major sins). Why does he say this is problematic?
This refutes the idea that major sins are punished identically in disbelief.
4
Analyze the Quranic proof
The author quotes Quran 29:13 and 7:38 to show that God singles out misleaders for double punishment. Reflect on how these verses support the distinction.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider the final remark: God punishes the disbeliever for all sins, but not the Muslim who holds the correct creed. How does this reinforce the main point?
This ties back to the earlier arguments about faith and accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الله جل ثناؤه أخبر أن الموعودة عقوبة الذين كفروا وأضلوا غيرهم ضعف عقوبة من كفر ولم يضل غيره ، ثم لو كان للكافر عقوبة غير الإضلال مثل عقوبة الإضلال لكان كل كافر عقوبته مضاعفة ؛ لأنه لا كافر إلا ومعه سوى الكفر - كبائر ، وقد خص الله بالمضاعفة المضلين / بقوله : وَلَيَحْمِلُنَّ أَثْقَالَهُمْ وأَثْقَالًا مَعَ أَنْقَالِهِم ، وقول الأتباع : رَبَّنَا هَوْلَاء أَضَلُّونَا ، وجعل لكل ضعفا ، فبطل أن يكون ذلك عقوبة الكبيرة ، بل هي لو كانت في الكفر كان أحق للضعف منه فى الإسلام للمثل ، ألا ترى أنه يعاقب الكافر بجميع الآثام من صغائر وكبائر ، ولا كذلك أمر من اعتقد دين الإسلام ، ولا قوة الا بالله
And also: that God, magnificent is His praise, informed that the promised punishment of those who disbelieve and mislead others is double the punishment of one who disbelieves but does not mislead others. Then, if the disbeliever had a punishment other than [that for] misguidance like that for misguidance, then every disbeliever would have double punishment, because there is no disbeliever except that with him besides kufr are major sins. And God specifically singled out the misleaders for double punishment by His words: 'They will surely bear their own burdens and [other] burdens along with their burdens' (29:13), and the words of the followers: 'Our Lord, these misled us' (7:38), and He made for each a double [punishment]. So it is invalid that [the punishment for major sins] be the punishment of the major sin; rather, if it were in kufr, it would be more deserving of the doubling in Islam for the like. Do you not see that He punishes the disbeliever for all sins, minor and major, but not so the one who adheres to the religion of Islam? And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 960

Commentary
This passage presents a third sub-argument within the second main argument. It uses Quranic evidence to prove that the punishment for kufr is distinct from punishment for major sins (kaba'ir). God promises double punishment to those who disbelieve and mislead others, compared to those who only disbelieve. If the punishment for kufr were the same as for misguidance, then every disbeliever would receive double punishment because all disbelievers have major sins alongside kufr. But God specifically singles out misleaders for double punishment, as shown in verses. Therefore the punishment for major sins cannot be the same as that for kufr. Moreover, if the punishment for major sins were included in kufr, it would be more fitting to double it in Islam for similar acts. Additionally, note that God punishes the disbeliever for all sins (minor and major), but not the Muslim who holds the Islamic creed—this further distinguishes the punishments.

Misconception: Major sins in a disbeliever result in double punishment.
Correction: The doubling is specific to misguidance, not to major sins per se; God singles out misleaders for double punishment, not all disbelievers.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Double punishment is reserved for those who combine disbelief with misguiding others, not for disbelievers in general.

Disbelief as a Permanent Creed vs. Occasional Sins
Maturidi compares kufr to a permanent sect or disposition, unlike occasional sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the difference in permanence between disbelief and major sins justifies their respective eternal and temporary punishments.

Core Idea
Disbelief is a fixed creed; major sins are passing acts.

This lesson presents a key reasoning from Imam Maturidi that distinguishes the nature of disbelief from major sins. By considering whether an act reflects a permanent disposition or a momentary lapse, we can understand why the punishments differ. The passage invites us to reflect on how the character of the deed determines its consequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi presents two categories: disbelief as a firmly held creed, and major sins as occasional acts.
The phrase 'yu'qad' (held firmly) is key—it implies permanence.
2
Identify the two categories
Locate the two things being compared: disbelief (kufr) and major sins (kaba'ir). Observe how each is described.
Disbelief is called a 'madhhab' (creed), while sins are described as momentary.
3
Understand the reasoning
Maturidi links permanence of the act to permanence of punishment. Because creeds are held forever, disbelief's punishment is eternal. Because sins are done for occasions, their punishment is temporary.
This is a logical argument based on the nature of the act itself.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: Does this argument make sense? How does it relate to other Islamic teachings on repentance and God's mercy?
The passage ends with 'And there is no power except with God.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الوجه الآخر من طريق الاعتبار أن الكفر مذهب يعقد ، والمذاهب] تعقد للأبد ، فعلى ذلك عقوبته ، وسائر الكبائر يفعل للأوقات ، وهو عند غلبة الشهوات لا للأبد ، فعلى ذلك عقوبتها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then the other face from the way of consideration: that disbelief is a creed that is held firmly, and creeds are held firmly forever; accordingly is its punishment. And other major sins are done for occasions, and they are at the time of overpowering of desires, not forever; accordingly is their punishment. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 961

Commentary
This passage introduces another argument (face) for the eternity of the punishment of kufr. It is a consideration based on the nature of disbelief versus sins. The author states that disbelief (kufr) is a creed (madhhab) that is firmly held (yu'qad), and creeds are held permanently. Therefore its punishment is eternal. In contrast, major sins (kaba'ir) are committed occasionally, prompted by the overpowering of desires, and are not permanent. Accordingly, their punishment is temporary. This argument distinguishes between a fixed belief system and transient acts.

D) Because disbelief is more harmful.

Key Takeaway
Disbelief is a permanent creed deserving eternal punishment, while major sins are occasional acts meriting temporary punishment.

Second Point: Disbelief is Intrinsically Evil
Maturidi argues that kufr is intrinsically evil and cannot be pardoned.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that disbelief is intrinsically evil and therefore unpardonable, distinguishing it from other sins.

Core Idea
Disbelief is evil in its very essence, making it unforgivable.

Imam Maturidi presents a sharp distinction between disbelief and other sins. While lesser sins can be forgiven because their evil is not inherent, disbelief is evil in its very essence. This passage establishes why kufr calls for an irreversible punishment in divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the key terms like qabih li-'aynihi, itlaq, and raf' al-hurma.
Pay attention to the contrast between kufr and other sins.
2
Identify the core distinction
Notice how the author contrasts kufr (intrinsically evil) with other sins (not intrinsically evil). Ask: What makes kufr different?
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Why does this distinction matter for understanding divine justice and forgiveness? Consider how it affects our view of God's mercy and punishment.
This connects to broader issues of sin and forgiveness in Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثانى أن الكفر قبيح لعينه ، لا يحتمل الإطلاق ورفع الحرمة ، فعلى ذلك عقوبته في الحكمة لا يحتمل الارتفاع والعفو عنه ، وسائر المأثم جائز رفع الحرمة عنها في العقل وإباحة ما له العقوبة ، فمثله عقوبته ، والله الموفق .
And the second: that disbelief is evil in its essence; it does not admit of being set loose and having its prohibition lifted. So accordingly its punishment in wisdom does not admit of being raised or being pardoned. And other sins, it is permissible in the intellect to lift the prohibition from them and to permit what has punishment; so its punishment is like it. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 961

Commentary
This passage presents a further point (labeled 'second' in the sequence) about the nature of kufr. The author states that disbelief (kufr) is intrinsically evil (qabih li-'aynihi) - evil in its very essence. Therefore it does not admit of being released (itlaq) or having its prohibition lifted (raf' al-hurma). Consequently, in wisdom, its punishment cannot be lifted or pardoned. In contrast, other sins (ma'athim) are such that reason permits the lifting of their prohibition and the permissibility of what they punish; thus their punishment is proportionate (like them). This underscores that kufr, due to its inherent evil, is unpardonable, while lesser sins may be subject to forgiveness.

Key Terms
قبيح لعينه
الإطلاق
رفع الحرمة
العفو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sins are equally evil and equally punishable.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between sins that are evil in themselves (kufr) and sins that are evil due to prohibition. Only kufr is intrinsically evil and thus unforgivable.

Checkpoint

A) Because kufr is evil in its essence, while other sins are not intrinsically evil.
B) Because God hates kufr more.
C) Because other sins are smaller.
D) Because kufr is a compound act.

Key Takeaway
Disbelief is intrinsically evil and, unlike other sins, cannot be pardoned because its evil is inherent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Third Point: Pardoning the Disbeliever Wastes Grace
Maturidi argues that forgiving a disbeliever is misplaced, as he sees evil as good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that pardoning a disbeliever is misplaced because the disbeliever considers his evil as good, thereby wasting pardon and nullifying grace.

Core Idea
Forgiveness requires recognition of wrongdoing; otherwise it is wasted.

Imam Maturidi presents the third argument in this section, focusing on the nature of divine forgiveness. He contends that pardoning a disbeliever is not appropriate because the disbeliever does not acknowledge the evil of disbelief. This sets a clear distinction between the disbeliever and other sinners who recognize their sin and the Bestower of grace.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms and the structure of the argument.
Pay attention to the contrast between the disbeliever and other sinners.
2
Identify the key terms
Look at the terms like 'al-'afw' (pardon), 'al-munkar' (evil), and 'al-ni'mah' (grace). Understand their meanings in context.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Understand the contrast
Maturidi contrasts the disbeliever, who sees his evil as right, with other sinners who know the Bestower. Grasp why this difference matters for forgiveness.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that forgiveness for the disbeliever is not wise, but for other sinners it is permissible. Think about the theological implications.
This shows divine wisdom in forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث أن العفو عن الكافر عفو في غير موضع العفو ؛ لأنه منكر ويرى ذلك حقا ، فيكون فى ذلك تضييع العفو وإبطال النعمة ، ولا كذلك أمر سائر المأثم ، بل يعرف صاحبها المنعم ، فله أعظم الموضع ، ولإكرامه أبين المحل ، فجائز المغفرة له والعفو عنه فى الحكمة ، وبالله المعونة
And the third: that pardoning the disbeliever is pardon in other than the place of pardon; because it is an evil and he sees it as right. So in that would be wasting of pardon and nullification of grace. And not so is the case of other sins; rather, their possessor knows the Bestower of grace, so he has the greatest place, and for honoring him is the clearest station. So it is permissible in wisdom to grant him forgiveness and pardon. And with God is assistance.
Source pages p. 961

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument. The author states that pardoning the disbeliever is pardon in the wrong place (fi ghayri mawdi' al-'afw). This is because disbelief (kufr) is an evil (munkar), and the disbeliever sees it as right (yara dhalika haqqa). Thus pardoning him would be a waste of pardon and a nullification of grace. In contrast, the committer of other sins (sā'ir al-ma'āthim) recognizes the Bestower of grace (al-mun'im), so he has the greatest status and clear station; therefore it is wise to grant him forgiveness and pardon. This distinguishes between the one who acknowledges the wrong and the one who insists it is right.

المغفرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's forgiveness is unconditional regardless of the recipient's attitude.
Correction: The passage shows that forgiveness depends on the recipient's recognition of wrongdoing; for the disbeliever who sees evil as good, pardon is wasted.

Checkpoint
Why is pardoning the disbeliever considered misplaced according to this argument?
A) Because the disbeliever considers his evil as right, so pardon would be wasted.
B) Because God does not forgive disbelief.
C) Because the disbeliever has too many sins.
D) Because pardon is only for Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Pardoning the disbeliever would waste grace because he sees his evil as right, unlike other sinners who recognize the Bestower.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever justify it to yourself, convincing yourself it's not that bad or that you have a valid reason? How does Maturidi's distinction between one who sees evil as right and one who recognizes his fault confront your own self-deception? Will you commit today to stop justifying your sins and instead acknowledge them as wrong before seeking forgiveness?
?
Reflect on a time you asked God for forgiveness while still harboring the belief that what you did was acceptable. According to this lesson, what does that say about the nature of your pardon request? Are you willing to ask God for the honesty to see your evil as evil, even when it hurts?
?
If pardoning the disbeliever wastes grace because he sees his evil as right, what does that imply about your own prayer for forgiveness? Do you truly see your sin as a transgression against the Bestower, or do you treat it lightly? What change must you make in your heart before your next istighfar?

Fourth Point: God's Grace and the Disbeliever's Ingratitude
Maturidi argues for eternal punishment despite God's grace.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the disbeliever's ingratitude, despite receiving God's immense favors, justifies eternal punishment, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite and Kharijite positions.

Core Idea
Divine grace magnifies ingratitude; eternal punishment is just.

This passage presents Maturidi's fourth argument, which is both theological and polemical. He builds a case from the principle of divine grace: God bestows immense favors on the disbeliever in matters of faith, yet the disbeliever, by his own choice, conceals or rejects that grace. Maturidi argues that such ingratitude, given the magnitude of God's blessings, cannot be mitigated by any sin—it calls for a just response that fits the offense. The argument culminates in a strong rejection of the Mu'tazilite and Kharijite view that God would not eternally punish such a person.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument from grace
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice how Maturidi begins by stating that God's favor (إحسان) is great, yet the disbeliever conceals it.
The passage is dense; pay attention to the phrases about the heart and the messengers.
2
Trace the logical flow
Identify the main premises: (1) God bestows grace, (2) the disbeliever responds with ingratitude, (3) God does not change grace until people change themselves, (4) eternal punishment is fitting.
3
Examine the prophetic tradition
The author cites a hadith about people joining those they love. Understand how this supports the argument that the disbeliever, despite loving the Prophet, cannot be rewarded in the same way as believers.
The tradition is used to show that love alone is not sufficient without faith.
4
Understand the refutation
The passage concludes by exalting God above the description of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij. Reflect on how the argument proves that God's attributes of mercy and justice are consistent with eternal punishment.
This refutes the sectarian view that God would be unjust to punish the disbeliever eternally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع أن يكون الله تعالى قد أحسن إليه فى الدين في الوقت الذي خفاه هو بفعله في أن جعل حقه أعظم فى قلبه من الدارين وانبيائه ورسله أجل فى صدره أن يحتمل نفسه الاستحقاق بشعرة من شعورهم أو الاستهانة بشيء من أمور أعدائه فيها قد اختاره و آثره من الخلان لله ، وكل من من دين الله أو الركون إلى أحد ذلك هو إحسان الله إليه وإنعامه عليه ، فلا يحتمل أن يضيع مننه ويغير نعمة بجفوة يعلم أن قدرها من الذنوب لا يبلغ حرفا مما لا يحصى من نعمه عليه وإحسانه إليه ، وهو يؤمن خلقه بأن لا يغير نعمة أنعمها على قوم حتى يغيروا ما بأنفسهم . وأن يضيع جميع ما أكرمهم به فمثل الذي ذكر ، وقد أنطق لسان رسوله أنه يدخل الجنة إلا من أبى ذلك ، ويجمع بين من ذكرت وبين أعدائه مع كثرة مجاهدته إياهم في نصر دينه وإعلاء كلمته ، وقد ختم عليه لا والله ما يفعل ذلك وهو الغنى الكريم وهو العفو الغفور وهو الرحيم الودود . مع ما جاءت البشارة عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فى لحوق العبيد لمن أحبوهم ، ثم كان الذي ذكر أحب رسول الله فيجعله قرين الشيطان ويحرمه زيارة رسوله ، جل ثناؤه عن هذا الوصف الذى وصفته به المعتزلة والخوارج ، على ظهور هذا الصنيع في جملتهم حتى لا يسلم من
And the fourth: that God, the Exalted, may have bestowed favor upon him in the religion at the time when he himself concealed it by his deed, in that He made His right greater in his heart than the two worlds, and His prophets and messengers more venerable in his breast than that he should deem himself deserving of a hair of their [rights] or belittle any of the affairs of His enemies, with what he has chosen and preferred from among the beloved for God. And everyone who [deviates] from the religion of God or inclines to any of that—that is God's favor upon him and His grace. So it is not possible that he should waste His favors and change [His] grace by a harshness that he knows its measure of sins does not reach a letter [of] what cannot be counted of His favors upon him and His grace. And He informs His creation that He does not change a grace He has bestowed upon a people until they change what is in themselves. And that He should waste all that He honored them with—such is like what was mentioned. And He has caused the tongue of His Messenger to speak that He will enter Paradise except those who refuse that, and He would gather between those mentioned and His enemies, despite their intense struggle on His behalf in supporting His religion and elevating His word. And He has sealed that—no, by God, He will not do that, while He is the Self-Sufficient, the Generous, the Pardoner, the Forgiving, the Merciful, the Loving. Along with the glad tidings from the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) about the joining of servants to those they love. Then, the one mentioned who loved the Messenger of God would become a companion of Satan and be deprived of visiting his Messenger. Exalted is He above this description which the Mu'tazila and Khawarij have described Him with, despite the appearance of this deed among their generality, so that no one is safe from...
Source pages p. 961

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth and longest argument. It is a complex argument from divine grace and human ingratitude. The author states that God has bestowed great favors on the disbeliever in the religion—making His right greater than the world and the Hereafter in the heart, and the prophets and messengers venerated such that the disbeliever should not deem himself worthy of a hair's breadth of their rights. Yet the disbeliever, by his own act, hides (خفى) this. The argument proceeds: anyone who deviates from God's religion or inclines to anything contrary is still the recipient of God's favor. It is impossible for God to waste His favors and change His grace due to a harshness (jafwa) from the disbeliever, which, even if it were a sin, would not amount to a letter of the countless blessings. God informs that He does not change a grace until people change what is in themselves. Then the author anticipates an objection: if God were to gather between the faithful and His enemies in the same place, despite the righteous ones' struggle, that would contradict God's attributes of generosity, pardon, mercy, and love. The author also cites the prophetic tradition that servants will join those they love. Then he vehemently denies that God would make a lover of the Messenger a companion of Satan and deprive him of visiting the Messenger. He exalts God above the description of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij who attribute such injustice to God.

Key Terms
الإحسان
النعمة
المنة
المعتزلة
الخوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the disbeliever being punished despite God's grace.
Correction: It actually shows that eternal punishment is consistent with God's attributes, because the disbeliever's ingratitude magnifies the offense.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about God's treatment of the disbeliever based on His grace?
A) God would not waste His favors and change grace due to a disbeliever's ingratitude, making eternal punishment fitting.
B) God will forgive the disbeliever because of His grace.
C) The disbeliever's good deeds outweigh his sins.
D) God's grace is unconditional.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument establishes that God's immense grace to the disbeliever makes his ingratitude inexcusable, and eternal punishment is not a violation of God's mercy but a just response consistent with His attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have received countless blessings from God—faith, health, family, provision. Have you ever used any of these blessings to disobey Him? How does your ingratitude compare to the disbeliever Maturidi describes who conceals God's favor by his own deed?
?
You claim to love the Prophet Muhammad, but are your actions consistent with that love? If you were to die today, would you be confident that your love is genuine enough to be joined with him, or would your deeds place you among those who are ungrateful for the favor of being guided?
?
When you sin, do you realize that you are changing God's grace by your own choice? What concrete step will you take today to truly appreciate and not 'conceal' the immense favor God has shown you in guiding you to Islam?
